<p><b>Alexander Hamilton</b> (January 11, 1755 or 1757 – July 12, 1804) was an American statesman and one of the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>. He was an influential interpreter and promoter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" title="U.S. Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a>, as well as the founder of the nation's financial system, the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_New_York_Post" title="The New York Post">The New York Post</a></i> newspaper. As the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a>, Hamilton was the main author of the economic policies of the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Presidency of George Washington">George Washington administration</a>. He took the lead in the funding of the states' debts by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_government" title="Federal government">Federal government</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">establishment of a national bank</a>, a system of tariffs, and friendly trade relations with Britain. His vision included a strong central government led by a vigorous executive branch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3–4</sup> a strong commercial economy, with a national bank and support for manufacturing, plus a strong military. This was challenged by Virginia agrarians <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> who formed a rival party. They favored strong states based in rural America and protected by state militias as opposed to a strong national army and navy. They denounced Hamilton as too friendly toward Britain and toward monarchy in general, and too oriented toward cities, business and banking.</p>
<p>Hamilton was born out of wedlock in <a href="/wiki/Charlestown,_Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Charlestown, Saint Kitts and Nevis">Charlestown</a>, British West Indies, to a mother of French Huguenot and British ancestry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8–9</sup> and a Scots father, James A. Hamilton, the fourth son of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> laird Alexander Hamilton of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grange,_Ayrshire" title="Grange, Ayrshire">Grange, Ayrshire</a>. Orphaned as a child by his mother's death and his father's abandonment, he was taken in by an older cousin, and later by a prosperous merchant family. He was recognized for his intelligence and talent, and sponsored by a group of wealthy local men to travel to New York City and pursue his education. Hamilton attended King's College (now <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>), choosing to stay in the Thirteen Colonies to seek his fortune.</p>
<p>After graduation, Hamilton played a major role in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. At the start of the war in 1775, he joined a militia company. In early 1776, he raised a provincial artillery company, to which he was appointed captain. He soon became the senior aide to General Washington, the American forces' commander-in-chief. Hamilton was dispatched by Washington on numerous missions to convey plans to his generals. After the war, Hamilton was elected as a representative to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> from New York. He resigned to practice law, and founded the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_New_York" title="Bank of New York">Bank of New York</a>.</p>
<p>Hamilton was among those dissatisfied with the weak national government. He led the <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">Annapolis Convention</a>, which successfully influenced Congress to issue a call for the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Philadelphia Convention</a> in order to create a new constitution. He was an active participant at <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, and he helped achieve ratification by writing 51 of the 85 installments of <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers"><i>The Federalist</i> Papers</a> which, to this day, are the single most important reference for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_interpretation" title="Constitutional interpretation">Constitutional interpretation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton became the leading cabinet member in the new government under President Washington. He was a nationalist who emphasized strong central government and successfully argued that the <a href="/wiki/Implied_powers" title="Implied powers">implied powers</a> of the Constitution provided the legal authority to fund the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_debt" title="National debt">national debt</a>, assume states' debts, and create the government-backed <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">Bank of the United States</a>. These programs were funded primarily by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tariff_in_American_history" title="Tariff in American history">a tariff on imports</a>, and later also by a highly controversial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiskey_tax" title="Whiskey tax">tax on whiskey</a>. To overcome localism Hamilton mobilized a nationwide network of friends of the government, especially bankers and businessmen, which became the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>. A major issue in the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">American two-party system</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a>, largely designed by Hamilton in 1794. It established friendly trade relations with Britain, to the chagrin of France and the supporters of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. Hamilton played a central role in the Federalist party, which dominated national and state politics until it lost the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">election of 1800</a> to Jefferson's <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>.</p>
<p>In 1795, he returned to the practice of law in New York. He tried to control the policies of <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">President Adams</a> (1797–1801). In 1798–99, Hamilton called for mobilization against <a href="/wiki/French_First_Republic" title="French First Republic">France</a> after the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a> and became <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">commander of a new army</a>, which he readied for war. However, the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> was never officially declared and did not involve army action, though it was hard-fought at sea. In the end, President Adams found a diplomatic solution that avoided a war with France. Hamilton's opposition to Adams' re-election helped cause his defeat in the 1800 election. Jefferson and <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> tied for the presidency in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral college</a> in 1801, and Hamilton helped to defeat Burr, whom he found unprincipled, and to elect Jefferson despite philosophical differences.</p>
<p>Hamilton continued his legal and business activities in New York City, and was active in ending the international slave trade. Vice President Burr ran for governor of New York State in 1804, and Hamilton crusaded against him as unworthy. Burr took offense and <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">challenged him to a duel</a>. Burr mortally wounded Hamilton, who died the next day.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_in_the_Caribbean">Childhood in the Caribbean</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg"><img alt="The Museum of Nevis History, Charlestown" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg/220px-The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg/330px-The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg/440px-The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Museum_of_Nevis_History_-_Alexander_Hamilton_birthplace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Hamilton House, Charlestown, Nevis. The current structure was rebuilt from the ruins of the house where Alexander Hamilton was born and lived as a young child.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:154px;max-width:154px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_of_grange.jpg"><img alt="Hamilton of grange" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="704" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Hamilton_of_grange.jpg/150px-Hamilton_of_grange.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Hamilton_of_grange.jpg/225px-Hamilton_of_grange.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Hamilton_of_grange.jpg/300px-Hamilton_of_grange.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Arms with crest and motto</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_of_Grange.png"><img alt="Hamilton Arms" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="507" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hamilton_of_Grange.png/150px-Hamilton_of_Grange.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hamilton_of_Grange.png/225px-Hamilton_of_Grange.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hamilton_of_Grange.png/300px-Hamilton_of_Grange.png 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Arms in color</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Arms of <a href="/wiki/Clan_Hamilton" title="Clan Hamilton">Hamilton</a> of Grange<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <b>·</b> ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laird" title="Laird">lairds</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kerelaw_Castle" title="Kerelaw Castle">Kerelaw Castle</a> or Grange. "gules, three cinquefoils ermine (<a href="/wiki/Clan_Hamilton" title="Clan Hamilton">Hamilton</a>), center a lion rampant, argent."</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Alexander Hamilton was born and spent part of his childhood in <a href="/wiki/Charlestown,_Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Charlestown, Saint Kitts and Nevis">Charlestown</a>, the capital of the island of <a href="/wiki/Nevis" title="Nevis">Nevis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Leeward_Islands" title="Leeward Islands">Leeward Islands</a>. (Nevis was one of the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a>.) Hamilton and his older brother James Jr. were born <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out-of-wedlock" title="Out-of-wedlock">out of wedlock</a> to Rachel Faucette, a married woman of British and French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> descent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> and James A. Hamilton, the fourth son of Scottish <a href="/wiki/Laird" title="Laird">laird</a> Alexander Hamilton of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grange,_Ayrshire" title="Grange, Ayrshire">Grange, Ayrshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randall1-7">[7]</a></sup> Speculation that Hamilton's mother was of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed_race" title="Mixed race">mixed race</a>, though persistent, is not substantiated by verifiable evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9,734–735</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owens_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-owens-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is not certain whether the year of Hamilton's birth was 1755 or 1757; most historical evidence after Hamilton's arrival in North America supports the idea that he was born in 1757, and many historians had accepted this birth date. But his early life in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> was recorded in documents which were first published in <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> in 1930; this evidence has caused historians since then to favor a birth year of 1755.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup> Hamilton listed his birth year as 1757 when he first arrived in the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a>. He celebrated his birthday on January 11. In later life, he tended to give his age only in round figures. <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">Probate</a> papers from St. Croix in 1768, after the death of Hamilton's mother, list him as 13 years old, a date that would support a birth year of 1755.</p>
<p>Historians have posited reasons for the different dates of birth being used. If 1755 is correct, Hamilton may have been trying to appear younger than his college classmates or perhaps wished to avoid standing out as older; if 1757 is correct, the probate document indicating a birth year of 1755 may have been in error, or Hamilton may have been attempting to pass as 13 in order to be more employable after his mother's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:149px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_alexander_hamilton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="147" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Young_alexander_hamilton.jpg" width="147"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_alexander_hamilton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamilton in his youth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>His mother moved with the young Hamilton to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Croix,_U.S._Virgin_Islands" title="Saint Croix, U.S. Virgin Islands">St. Croix</a> in the Virgin Islands, then ruled by <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. She had been married previously to Johann Michael Lavien of St. Croix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:10</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Randall1-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[note 1]</a></sup> Faucette left her husband and first son Peter, traveling to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Kitts" title="St. Kitts">St. Kitts</a> in 1750 where she met James Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:12</sup> Hamilton and Faucette moved together to her birthplace Nevis, where she had inherited property from her father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup></p>
<p>James Hamilton abandoned Rachel Faucette and their sons, allegedly to "spar[e] [her] a charge of <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a>... after finding out that her first husband intend[ed] to divorce her under Danish law on grounds of adultery and desertion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Randall1-7">[7]</a></sup> Thereafter, she supported her children in St. Croix, keeping a small store in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christiansted" title="Christiansted">Christiansted</a>. She contracted a severe fever and died on February 19, 1768, 1:02 am, leaving Hamilton orphaned. This may have had severe emotional consequences for him, even by the standards of an 18th-century childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Probate_court" title="Probate court">probate court</a>, Faucette's "first husband seized her estate"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall1_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Randall1-7">[7]</a></sup> and obtained the few valuables that she had owned, including some household silver. Many items were <a href="/wiki/Auction" title="Auction">auctioned</a> off, but a friend purchased the family's books and returned them to Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:25</sup></p>
<p>Hamilton became a clerk at local import-export firm Beekman and Cruger which traded with <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>; he was left in charge of the firm for five months in 1771 while the owner was at sea. He and James Jr. were briefly taken in by their cousin Peter Lytton; however, Lytton committed suicide. The brothers were subsequently separated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup> James apprenticed with a local carpenter, while Alexander was given a home by Nevis merchant Thomas Stevens. According to <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a>, some evidence suggests that Stevens may have been Alexander Hamilton's biological father; his son <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stevens_(diplomat)" title="Edward Stevens (diplomat)">Edward Stevens</a> became a close friend of Hamilton. The two boys were described as looking much alike. Both were fluent in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and shared similar interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27–30</sup></p>
<p>While Hamilton continued clerking, he remained an avid reader and later developed an interest in writing. He began to desire a life outside the island where he lived. He wrote a letter to his father that was a detailed account of a hurricane which had devastated Christiansted on August 30, 1772. Hugh Knox, a minister and journalist, published the letter in the <i>Royal Danish-American Gazette.</i> Chernow found the letter astounding for two reasons; first, that "for all its bombastic excesses, it does seem wondrous [that a] self-educated clerk could write with such verve and gusto," and second, that a teenage boy produced an apocalyptic "fire-and-brimstone sermon" viewing the hurricane as a "divine rebuke to human vanity and pomposity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:37</sup> The essay impressed community leaders, who collected a fund to send Hamilton to the North American colonies for his education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordon_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-gordon-12">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2757" data-file-width="3831" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/220px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/330px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG/440px-Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_statue_at_Columbia_University_IMG_0958.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Hamilton outside Hamilton Hall overlooking Hamilton Lawn at his alma mater, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> in New York City</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> denied membership to Alexander and James Hamilton, Jr.—and education in the church school—because their parents were not legally married. They received "individual tutoring"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup> and classes in a private school led by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jewish</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headmistress" title="Headmistress">headmistress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> Alexander supplemented his education with a family library of 34 books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:34</sup></p>
<p>In October 1772, he arrived <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">on the mainland</a> and began learning fundamental subjects missing from his education. He attended the Elizabethtown Academy, a <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar school</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabethtown,_New_Jersey" title="Elizabethtown, New Jersey">Elizabethtown, New Jersey</a>. In 1773, he studied with <a href="/wiki/Francis_Barber_(Colonel)" title="Francis Barber (Colonel)">Francis Barber</a> at Elizabethtown in preparation for college work. He came under the influence of <a href="/wiki/William_Livingston" title="William Livingston">William Livingston</a>, a leading intellectual and revolutionary, with whom he lived for a time at his <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Hall_(New_Jersey)" title="Liberty Hall (New Jersey)">Liberty Hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> Hamilton entered <a href="/wiki/The_King%27s_College_(New_York)" title="The King's College (New York)">King's College</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>) in the autumn of 1773 "as a private student", and officially matriculated in May 1774.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> His friend Robert Troup spoke glowingly of Hamilton's ability to clearly and concisely explain the rights and reasons that the patriots had in their case against the British, in what is credited as his first public appearance on July 6, 1774 at the liberty pole at King's College.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Hamilton, Troup, and four other undergraduates formed an unnamed literary society that is regarded as a precursor of the <a href="/wiki/Philolexian_Society" title="Philolexian Society">Philolexian Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:53</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Church of England clergyman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_Seabury_(1729%E2%80%931796)" title="Samuel Seabury (1729–1796)">Samuel Seabury</a> published a series of pamphlets promoting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loyalist" title="Loyalist">Loyalist</a> cause in 1774, to which Hamilton responded anonymously with his first political writings, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Full_Vindication_of_the_Measures_of_Congress" title="A Full Vindication of the Measures of Congress">A Full Vindication of the Measures of Congress</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Farmer_Refuted" title="The Farmer Refuted">The Farmer Refuted</a>.</i> Seabury essentially tried to provoke fear in the colonies, and his main objective was to stop the potential union among the colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Hamilton published two additional pieces attacking the <a href="/wiki/Quebec_Act" title="Quebec Act">Quebec Act</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> and may have also authored the fifteen anonymous installments of "The Monitor" for Holt's <i>New York Journal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> Hamilton was a supporter of the Revolutionary cause at this pre-war stage, although he did not approve of mob reprisals against Loyalists. On May 10, 1775, Hamilton won credit for saving his college president <a href="/wiki/Myles_Cooper" title="Myles Cooper">Myles Cooper</a>, a Loyalist, from an angry mob by speaking to the crowd long enough for Cooper to escape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="During_the_Revolutionary_War">During the Revolutionary War</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="450" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg/170px-Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg/255px-Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg/340px-Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_1757_1804_hi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Alexander Hamilton in the Uniform of the New York Artillery</i> by <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_Chappel" title="Alonzo Chappel">Alonzo Chappel</a> (1828–1887)</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_military_career">Early military career</span></h3>
<p>In 1775, after the first engagement of American troops with the British at <a href="/wiki/Battles_of_Lexington_and_Concord" title="Battles of Lexington and Concord">Lexington and Concord</a>, Hamilton and other King's College students joined a New York volunteer <a href="/wiki/Militia_(United_States)" title="Militia (United States)">militia</a> company called the Corsicans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> later renamed or reformed as the <a href="/wiki/Hearts_of_Oak_(New_York_militia)" title="Hearts of Oak (New York militia)">Hearts of Oak</a>.</p>
<p>He drilled with the company, before classes, in the graveyard of nearby <a href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Chapel" title="St. Paul's Chapel">St. Paul's Chapel</a>. Hamilton studied military history and tactics on his own and was soon recommended for promotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> Under fire from <a href="/wiki/HMS_Asia_(1764)" title="HMS Asia (1764)">HMS <i>Asia</i></a>, he led a successful raid for British cannons in <a href="/wiki/Battery_Park" title="Battery Park">the Battery</a>, the capture of which resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Hearts_of_Oak_(New_York_militia)" title="Hearts of Oak (New York militia)">Hearts of Oak</a> becoming an artillery company thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:13</sup></p>
<p>Through his connections with influential <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_York" title="Province of New York">New York</a> patriots such as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McDougall" title="Alexander McDougall">Alexander McDougall</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a>, Hamilton raised the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Provincial_Company_of_Artillery" title="New York Provincial Company of Artillery">New York Provincial Company of Artillery</a> of sixty men in 1776, and was elected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Captain_(Continental_Army)" title="Captain (Continental Army)">captain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:72</sup> It took part in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Campaign" title="New York Campaign">campaign of 1776</a> around New York City, particularly at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_White_Plains" title="Battle of White Plains">Battle of White Plains</a>; at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a>, it was stationed at the high point of town, the meeting of the present Warren and Broad streets, to keep the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldiers)" title="Hessian (soldiers)">Hessians</a> pinned in the Trenton Barracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stryker158_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-stryker158-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ketchum361-64_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ketchum361-64-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton participated in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Princeton" title="Battle of Princeton">Battle of Princeton</a> on January 3, 1777. After an initial setback, Washington rallied the American troops and led them in a successful charge against the British forces. After making a brief stand, the British fell back, some leaving Princeton, and others taking up refuge in <a href="/wiki/Nassau_Hall" title="Nassau Hall">Nassau Hall</a>. Hamilton brought three cannons up and had them fire upon the building. Then some Americans rushed the front door, and broke it down. The British subsequently put a white flag outside one of the windows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ketchum361-64_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-ketchum361-64-26">[25]</a></sup> Exactly 194 British soldiers walked out of the building and laid down their arms, thus ending the battle in an American victory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stryker290_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-stryker290-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="George_Washington.27s_staff">George Washington's staff</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton was invited to become an aide to <a href="/wiki/William_Alexander,_Lord_Stirling" title="William Alexander, Lord Stirling">William Alexander, Lord Stirling</a> and one other general, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Nathanael_Greene" title="Nathanael Greene">Nathanael Greene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McDougall" title="Alexander McDougall">Alexander McDougall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> He declined these invitations, believing his best chance for improving his station in life was glory on the battlefield. Hamilton eventually received an invitation he felt he could not refuse: to serve as Washington's <a href="/wiki/Washington%27s_Aides-de-Camp" title="Washington's Aides-de-Camp">aide</a>, with the rank of <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_colonel_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant colonel (United States)">Lieutenant Colonel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> Washington felt, "Aides de camp are persons in whom entire confidence must be placed and it requires men of abilities to execute the duties with propriety and dispatch."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton served for four years as Washington's chief staff aide. He handled letters to Congress, state governors, and the most powerful generals in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a>; he drafted many of Washington's orders and letters at the latter's direction; he eventually issued orders from Washington over Hamilton's own signature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-16"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:90</sup>Hamilton was involved in a wide variety of high-level duties, including <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomacy</a>, and negotiation with senior army officers as Washington's emissary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1978" data-file-width="3000" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/220px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/330px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg/440px-Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Lord_Cornwallis" title="Surrender of Lord Cornwallis">Surrender of Cornwallis at Yorktown</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>, oil on canvas, 1820. Colonel Hamilton is depicted as the leftmost of the four standing men on the right edge of the painting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the war, Hamilton became close friends with several fellow officers. His letters to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> and to <a href="/wiki/John_Laurens" title="John Laurens">John Laurens</a>, employing the <a href="/wiki/Sentimental_novel" title="Sentimental novel">sentimental</a> literary conventions of the late eighteenth century and alluding to Greek history and mythology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> have been read by <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Ned_Katz" title="Jonathan Ned Katz">Jonathan Ned Katz</a> as revealing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homosocial" title="Homosocial">homosocial</a> or perhaps homosexual relationship, but few historians agree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>While on Washington's staff, Hamilton long sought command and a return to active combat. As the war drew nearer to an end, he knew that opportunities for military glory were diminishing. In February 1781, Hamilton was mildly reprimanded by Washington and used this as an excuse to resign his staff position. He asked Washington and others for a field command. This continued until early July 1781, when Hamilton submitted a letter to Washington with his <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">commission</a> enclosed, "thus tacitly threatening to resign if he didn't get his desired command." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-17"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:159</sup></p>
<p>On July 31, 1781, Washington relented and assigned Hamilton as commander of a New York <a href="/wiki/Light_infantry" title="Light infantry">light infantry</a> battalion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> In the planning for the assault on <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Yorktown" title="Siege of Yorktown">Yorktown</a>, Hamilton was given command of three <a href="/wiki/Battalion" title="Battalion">battalions</a>, which were to fight in conjunction with the allied <a href="/wiki/French_Army" title="French Army">French troops</a> in taking <a href="/wiki/Redoubt" title="Redoubt">Redoubts</a> No. 9 and No. 10 of the British fortifications at Yorktown. Hamilton and his battalions fought bravely and took Redoubt No. 10 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayonets" title="Bayonets">bayonets</a> in a nighttime action, as planned. The French also fought bravely, suffered heavy casualties, and took Redoubt No. 9. These actions forced the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British</a> surrender of an entire army at <a href="/wiki/Yorktown,_Virginia" title="Yorktown, Virginia">Yorktown, Virginia</a>, effectively ending their major <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">British</a> military operations in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congress_of_the_Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</span></h3>
<p>After the Battle of Yorktown, Hamilton resigned his commission. He was appointed in July 1782 to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> as a New York representative for the term beginning in November 1782.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> Before his appointment to Congress in 1782, Hamilton was already sharing his criticisms of Congress. He expressed these criticisms in his letter to James Duane dated September 3, 1780. In this letter he wrote, "The fundamental defect is a want of power in Congress…the confederation itself is defective and requires to be altered; it is neither fit for war, nor peace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>While on Washington's staff, Hamilton had become frustrated with the decentralized nature of the wartime Continental Congress, particularly its dependence upon the states for voluntary financial support. Under the Articles of Confederation, Congress had no power to collect taxes or to demand money from the states. This lack of a stable source of funding had made it difficult for the Continental Army both to obtain its necessary provisions and to pay its soldiers. During the war, and for some time after, Congress obtained what funds it could from subsidies from the King of France, from aid requested from the several states (which were often unable or unwilling to contribute), and from European loans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>An amendment to the Articles had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burke_(North_Carolina)" title="Thomas Burke (North Carolina)">Thomas Burke</a>, in February 1781, to give Congress the power to collect a 5% impost, or duty on all imports, but this required ratification by all states; securing its passage as law proved impossible after it was rejected by Rhode Island in November 1782. Madison joined Hamilton in persuading Congress to send a delegation to persuade Rhode Island to change its mind. Their report recommending the delegation argued the federal government needed not just some level of financial autonomy, but also the ability to make laws that superseded those of the individual states. Hamilton transmitted a letter arguing that Congress already had the power to tax, since it had the power to fix the sums due from the several states; but Virginia's <a href="/wiki/Rescission" title="Rescission">rescission</a> of its own ratification ended the Rhode Island negotiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-18"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:176</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congress_and_the_army">Congress and the army</span></h3>
<p>While Hamilton was in Congress, discontented soldiers began to pose a danger to the young United States. Most of the army was then posted at <a href="/wiki/Newburgh,_New_York" title="Newburgh, New York">Newburgh</a>, New York. Those in the army were paying for much of their own supplies, and they had not been paid in eight months. Furthermore, the Continental officers had been promised, in May 1778, after <a href="/wiki/Valley_Forge" title="Valley Forge">Valley Forge</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pension" title="Pension">pension</a> of half their pay when they were discharged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> By the early 1780s, due to the structure of the government under the Articles of Confederation, it had no power to tax to either raise revenue or pay its soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker15_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker15-43">[42]</a></sup> In 1782 after several months without pay, a group of officers organized to send a delegation to lobby Congress, led by Capt. <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McDougall" title="Alexander McDougall">Alexander McDougall</a>. The officers had three demands: the Army's pay, their own pensions, and commutation of those pensions into a lump-sum payment if Congress were unable to afford the half-salary pensions for life. Congress rejected the proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker15_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker15-43">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several Congressmen, including Hamilton, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morris_(financier)" title="Robert Morris (financier)">Robert Morris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gouverneur_Morris" title="Gouverneur Morris">Gouverneur Morris</a>, attempted to use this <a href="/wiki/Newburgh_Conspiracy" title="Newburgh Conspiracy">Newburgh Conspiracy</a> as leverage to secure support from the states and in Congress for funding of the national government. They encouraged MacDougall to continue his aggressive approach, threatening unknown consequences if their demands were not met, and defeated proposals that would have resolved the crisis without establishing general federal taxation: that the states assume the debt to the army, or that an <a href="/wiki/Tax#Tariff" title="Tax">impost</a> be established dedicated to the sole purpose of paying that debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellis141_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ellis141-44">[43]</a></sup> Hamilton suggested using the Army's claims to prevail upon the states for the proposed national funding system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> The Morrises and Hamilton contacted Knox to suggest he and the officers defy civil authority, at least by not disbanding if the army were not satisfied; Hamilton wrote Washington to suggest that Hamilton covertly "take direction" of the officers' efforts to secure redress, to secure continental funding but keep the army within the limits of moderation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-19"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:177</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Washington wrote Hamilton back, declining to introduce the army;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> after the crisis had ended, he warned of the dangers of using the army as leverage to gain support for the national funding plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-20"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:177–80</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 15, Washington defused the Newburgh situation by giving a speech to the officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ellis141_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-ellis141-44">[43]</a></sup> Congress ordered the Army officially disbanded in April 1783. In the same month, Congress passed a new measure for a twenty-five-year impost—which Hamilton voted against<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>—that again required the consent of all the states; it also approved a commutation of the officers' pensions to five years of full pay. Rhode Island again opposed these provisions, and Hamilton's robust assertions of national prerogatives in his previous letter were widely held to be excessive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1783, a different group of disgruntled soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster, Pennsylvania</a>, sent Congress a petition demanding their back pay. When they began to march toward Philadelphia, Congress charged Hamilton and two others with intercepting the mob.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-21"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:180</sup> Hamilton requested militia from Pennsylvania's Supreme Executive Council, but was turned down. Hamilton instructed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Assistant_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Assistant Secretary of War">Assistant Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Jackson_(secretary)" title="William Jackson (secretary)">William Jackson</a> to intercept the men. Jackson was unsuccessful. The mob arrived in Philadelphia, and the soldiers proceeded to harangue Congress for their pay. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Continental_Congress" title="President of the Continental Congress">President of the Continental Congress</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Dickinson" title="John Dickinson">John Dickinson</a>, feared that the Pennsylvania state militia was unreliable, and refused its help. Hamilton argued that Congress ought to adjourn to <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton, New Jersey</a>. Congress agreed, and relocated there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-22"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:182</sup> Frustrated with the weakness of the central government, Hamilton while in Princeton drafted a call to revise the Articles of Confederation. This resolution contained many features of the future U.S. Constitution, including a strong federal government with the ability to collect taxes and raise an army. It also included the separation of powers into the Executive, Legislative, and Judicial branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-23"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">: 183</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_New_York">Return to New York</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton resigned from Congress, and in July 1783 was authorized to practice law in New York after several months of self-directed education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-24"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:160</sup> He practiced law in New York City in partnership with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Harison" title="Richard Harison">Richard Harison</a>. He specialized in defending Tories and British subjects, as in <i><a href="/wiki/Rutgers_v._Waddington" title="Rutgers v. Waddington">Rutgers v. Waddington</a></i>, in which he defeated a claim for damages done to a brewery by the Englishmen who held it during the military occupation of New York. He pleaded for the Mayor's Court to interpret state law consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">1783 Treaty of Paris</a> which had ended the Revolutionary War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-25"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:197–99</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:64–69</sup></p>
<p>In 1784, he founded the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_New_York" title="Bank of New York">Bank of New York</a> which became one of the longest operating banks in American history, it stayed in business for over 220 years before it merged with another bank in 2007. Hamilton was one of the men who restored King's College, which had been suspended since 1776 and severely damaged during the War, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_College_of_Columbia_University" title="Columbia College of Columbia University">Columbia College</a>. Long dissatisfied with the weak Articles of Confederation, he played a major leadership role at the <a href="/wiki/Annapolis_Convention_(1786)" title="Annapolis Convention (1786)">Annapolis Convention</a> in 1786. He drafted its resolution for a constitutional convention, and in doing so brought his longtime desire to have a more powerful, more financially independent federal government one step closer to reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1786, at the suggestion of either <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Gorham" title="Nathaniel Gorham">Nathaniel Gorham</a>, then-President of the Continental Congress,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_von_Steuben" title="Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben">Friedrich Wilhelm von Steuben</a>, the Prussian general who served in the Continental Army,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Hamilton invited <a href="/wiki/Prince_Henry_of_Prussia_(1726%E2%80%931802)" title="Prince Henry of Prussia (1726–1802)">Prince Henry of Prussia</a> to become President<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/King" title="King">King</a> of the United States, but the offer was revoked before the prince could make a reply.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution_and_The_Federalist_Papers">Constitution and <i>The Federalist</i> Papers</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers">The Federalist Papers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1095" data-file-width="831" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg/170px-Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg/255px-Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg/340px-Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-Alexander-LOC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamilton shortly after the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitutional_Convention_and_ratification_of_the_Constitution">Constitutional Convention and ratification of the Constitution</span></h3>
<p>In 1787, Hamilton served as assemblyman from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_County" title="New York County">New York County</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Legislature" title="New York State Legislature">New York State Legislature</a> and was chosen as a delegate for the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a> by his father-in-law Philip Schuyler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:191</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Even though Hamilton had been a leader in calling for a new Constitutional Convention, his direct influence at the Convention itself was quite limited. Governor <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a>'s faction in the New York legislature had chosen New York's other two delegates, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Lansing,_Jr." title="John Lansing, Jr.">John Lansing, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Yates_(politician)" title="Robert Yates (politician)">Robert Yates</a>, and both of them opposed Hamilton's goal of a strong national government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-26"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:227–28</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morton15_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morton15-57">[56]</a></sup> Thus, whenever the other two members of the New York delegation were present, they decided New York's vote, to ensure that there were no major alterations to the Articles of Confederation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:195</sup></p>
<p>Early in the Convention he made a speech proposing a President-for-Life; it had no effect upon the deliberations of the convention. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Convention#Hamilton.27s_plan" title="Philadelphia Convention">He proposed</a> to have an elected President and elected <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senators</a> who would serve for life, contingent upon "good behavior" and subject to removal for corruption or abuse; this idea contributed later to the hostile view of Hamilton as a monarchist sympathizer, held by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-27"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:232</sup> According to Madison's notes, Hamilton said in regards to the executive, "The English model was the only good one on this subject. The hereditary interest of the king was so interwoven with that of the nation, and his personal emoluments so great, that he was placed above the danger of being corrupted from abroad…Let one executive be appointed for life who dares execute his powers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Hamilton argued, "And let me observe that an executive is less dangerous to the liberties of the people when in office during life than for seven years. It may be said this constitutes as an elective monarchy…But by making the executive subject to impeachment, the term 'monarchy' cannot apply…"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> During the convention, Hamilton constructed a draft for the Constitution based on the convention debates, but he never presented it. This draft had most of the features of the actual Constitution. In this draft, the Senate was to be elected in proportion to the population, being two-fifths the size of the House, and the President and Senators were to be elected through complex multistage elections, in which chosen electors would elect smaller bodies of electors; they would hold office for life, but were removable for misconduct. The President would have an absolute veto. The <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> was to have immediate jurisdiction over all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lawsuits" title="Lawsuits">lawsuits</a> involving the United States, and state governors were to be appointed by the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of the Convention, Hamilton was still not content with the final Constitution, but signed it anyway as a vast improvement over the Articles of Confederation, and urged his fellow delegates to do so also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Since the other two members of the New York delegation, Lansing and Yates, had already withdrawn, Hamilton was the only New York signer to the United States Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:206</sup> He then took a highly active part in the successful campaign for the document's ratification in New York in 1788, which was a crucial step in its national ratification. He first used the popularity of the Constitution by the masses to compel George Clinton to sign, but was unsuccessful. The state convention in Poughkeepsie in June 1788 pitted Hamilton, Jay, <a href="/wiki/James_Duane" title="James Duane">James Duane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Le_Roy_Livingston" title="Robert Le Roy Livingston">Robert Livingston</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Morris_(U.S._politician)" title="Richard Morris (U.S. politician)">Richard Morris</a> against the Clintonian faction led by <a href="/wiki/Melancton_Smith" title="Melancton Smith">Melancton Smith</a>, Lansing, Yates, and <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Livingston_(legislator)" title="Gilbert Livingston (legislator)">Gilbert Livingston</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denboer15_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denboer15-62">[61]</a></sup> Hamilton's faction were against any conditional ratification, under the impression that New York would not be accepted into the Union, while Clinton's faction wanted to amend the Constitution, while maintaining the state's right to secede if their attempts failed. During the state convention, New Hampshire and Virginia becoming the ninth and tenth states to ratify the Constitution, respectively, had ensured any adjournment would not happen and a compromise would have to be reached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denboer15_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Denboer15-62">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan15_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan15-63">[62]</a></sup> Hamilton's arguments used for the ratifications were largely iterations of work from <i>The Federalist</i> Papers, and Smith eventually went for ratification, though it was more out of necessity than Hamilton's rhetoric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaplan15_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kaplan15-63">[62]</a></sup> The vote in the state constitution was ratified 30 to 27, on July 26, 1788.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1788, Hamilton served yet another term in what proved to be the last session of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>. When the term of Philip Schuyler was up in 1791, elected in his place was the attorney general of New York, one <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. Hamilton blamed Burr for this result, and ill characterizations of Burr appear in his correspondence thereafter. The two men did work together from time to time thereafter on various projects, including Hamilton's army of 1798 and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_Water_Company" title="Manhattan Water Company">Manhattan Water Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Federalist_Papers"><i>The Federalist</i> Papers</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton recruited John Jay and James Madison to write a series of essays defending the proposed Constitution, now known as <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers"><i>The Federalist</i> Papers</a>, and made the largest contribution to that effort, writing 51 of 85 essays published (Madison wrote 29, Jay only five). Hamilton supervised the entire project, enlisted the participants, wrote the majority of the essays, and oversaw the publication. During the project each person was responsible for their areas of expertise; Jay covered foreign relations, Madison covered the history of republics and confederacies, along with the anatomy of the new government and Hamilton covered the branches of government most pertinent to him: the executive and judicial branches, with some aspects of the Senate, as well as covering military matters and taxation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-28"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:247–48</sup> The papers first appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent_Journal" title="The Independent Journal">The Independent Journal</a></i> on October 27, 1787.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-29"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:247</sup></p>
<p>Hamilton wrote the first paper signed as <a href="/wiki/Publius_(praenomen)" title="Publius (praenomen)">Publius</a>, and all of the subsequent papers were signed under the name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:210</sup> Jay wrote the next four papers to elaborate on the confederation's weakness and the need for unity against foreign aggression and against splitting into rival confederacies, and, except for Number 64, was not further involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-30"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:253</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:211</sup> Hamilton's highlights included discussion that although republics have been culpable for disorders in the past, advances in the "science of politics" had fostered principles that ensured that those abuses could be prevented, such as the division of powers, legislative checks and balances, an independent judiciary, and legislators that were represented by electors (Numbers 7–9).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-31"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:254</sup> Hamilton also wrote an extensive defense of the constitution (No. 23–36), and discussed the Senate and executive and judicial branches in Numbers 65–85. Hamilton and Madison worked to describe the anarchic state of the confederation in numbers 15–22, and have been described as not being entirely different in thought during this time period in contrast to their stark opposition later in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-32"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:252–55</sup> Subtle differences appeared with the two when discussing the necessity of standing armies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-33"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:257</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconciliation_between_New_York_and_Vermont">Reconciliation between New York and Vermont</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vermont_Republic" title="Vermont Republic">Vermont Republic</a></div>
<p>In 1764 King George III had ruled in favor of New York in a dispute between New York and New Hampshire over the region that later became the state of <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. New York then refused to recognize claims to property derived from grants by New Hampshire governor <a href="/wiki/Benning_Wentworth" title="Benning Wentworth">Benning Wentworth</a> during the preceding 15 years when the territory had been governed as a de facto part of New Hampshire. Consequently, the people of the disputed territory, called the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_Grants" title="New Hampshire Grants">New Hampshire Grants</a>, resisted the enforcement of New York's laws within the Grants. Indeed, <a href="/wiki/Ethan_Allen" title="Ethan Allen">Ethan Allen</a>'s militia called the <a href="/wiki/Green_Mountain_Boys" title="Green Mountain Boys">Green Mountain Boys</a>, noted for successes in the war against the British in 1775, was originally formed for the purpose of resisting the colonial government of New York. In 1777 the statesmen of the Grants declared it a <a href="/wiki/Vermont_Republic" title="Vermont Republic">separate state to be called Vermont</a>, and by early 1778 had erected a state government. During 1777–1785, Vermont was repeatedly denied representation in the Continental Congress, largely because New York insisted that Vermont was legally a part of New York. Vermont took the position that because its petitions for <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_the_Union" title="Admission to the Union">admission to the Union</a> were denied, it was not a part of the United States, not subject to Congress, and at liberty to negotiate separately with the British. The latter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haldimand_negotiations" title="Haldimand negotiations">Haldimand negotiations</a> led to some exchanges of prisoners of war. The peace treaty of 1783 that ended the war included Vermont within the boundaries of the United States. On March 2, 1784, Governor George Clinton of New York asked Congress to declare war for the purpose of overthrowing the government of Vermont, but Congress made no decision.</p>
<p>By 1787 the government of New York had almost entirely given up plans to subjugate Vermont, but still claimed jurisdiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> As a member of the legislature of New York, Hamilton argued forcefully and at length in favor of a bill to recognize the sovereignty of the State of Vermont, against numerous objections to its constitutionality and policy. Consideration of the bill was deferred to a later date. In 1787 through 1789 Hamilton exchange letters with <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Chipman" title="Nathaniel Chipman">Nathaniel Chipman</a>, a lawyer representing Vermont. In 1788 the new Constitution of the United States went into effect, with its plan to replace the unicameral Continental Congress with a new Congress consisting of a Senate and a House of Representatives. Hamilton wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>One of the first subjects of deliberation with the new Congress will be the independence of Kentucky [at that time still a part of Virginia], for which the southern states will be anxious. The northern will be glad to find a counterpoise in Vermont.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1790 the New York legislature decided to give up New York's claim to Vermont if Congress decided to admit Vermont to the Union and if negotiations between New York and Vermont on the boundary between the two states were successfully concluded. In 1790 negotiators discussed not only boundary, but also financial compensation of New York land-grantees whose grants Vermont refused to recognize because they conflicted with earlier grants from New Hampshire. Compensation in the amount of 30,000 Spanish dollars was agreed to, and Vermont was admitted to the Union in 1791.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_the_Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 260px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -62px; left: -48px; width: 320px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HAMILTON,_Alexander-Treasury_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg"><img alt="Bureau of Engraving and Printing portrait of Hamilton as Secretary of the Treasury" data-file-height="8472" data-file-width="6912" height="392" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/320px-HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/480px-HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/640px-HAMILTON%2C_Alexander-Treasury_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="320"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HAMILTON,_Alexander-Treasury_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:HAMILTON, Alexander-Treasury (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:HAMILTON, Alexander-Treasury (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<center><a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">Bureau of Engraving and Printing</a> portrait of Hamilton as Secretary of the Treasury</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>President George Washington appointed Hamilton as the first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">United States Secretary of the Treasury</a> on September 11, 1789. He left office on the last day of January 1795. Much of the structure of the government of the United States was worked out in those five years, beginning with the structure and function of the cabinet itself. Biographer <a href="/wiki/Forrest_McDonald" title="Forrest McDonald">Forrest McDonald</a> argues that Hamilton saw his office, like that of the British <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a>, as the equivalent of a Prime Minister; Hamilton would oversee his colleagues under the elective reign of George Washington. Washington did request Hamilton's advice and assistance on matters outside the purview of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">Treasury Department</a>. In 1791, while Secretary, Hamilton was elected a Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-67">[66]</a></sup> Hamilton submitted various financial reports to Congress. Among these are the <a href="/wiki/First_Report_on_the_Public_Credit" title="First Report on the Public Credit">First Report on the Public Credit</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operations_of_the_Act_Laying_Duties_on_Imports" title="Operations of the Act Laying Duties on Imports">Operations of the Act Laying Duties on Imports</a>, <a href="/wiki/Report_on_a_National_Bank" title="Report on a National Bank">Report on a National Bank</a>, On the Establishment of a Mint, <a href="/wiki/Report_on_Manufactures" title="Report on Manufactures">Report on Manufactures</a>, and the Report on a Plan for the Further Support of Public Credit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> So, the great enterprise in Hamilton's project of an administrative republic is the establishment of stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Report_on_Public_Credit">Report on Public Credit</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Report_on_Public_Credit" title="First Report on Public Credit">First Report on Public Credit</a></div>
<p>Before the adjournment of the House in September 1789, they requested Hamilton to make a report on suggestions to improve the public credit by January 1790.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray121_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray121-70">[69]</a></sup> Hamilton had written to Robert Morris as early as 1781 that fixing the public credit will win their objective of independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray121_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray121-70">[69]</a></sup> The sources that Hamilton used ranged from Frenchmen such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Necker" title="Jacques Necker">Jacques Necker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> to British writers such as <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malachy_Postlethwayt" title="Malachy Postlethwayt">Malachy Postlethwayt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-34"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:296</sup> While writing the report he also sought out suggestions from contemporaries such as <a href="/wiki/John_Witherspoon" title="John Witherspoon">John Knox Witherspoon</a>, and Madison. Although they agreed on additional taxes such as distilleries and duties on imported liquors and land taxes, Madison feared that the securities from the government debt would fall in foreign hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-35"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:121</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-5"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:244–45</sup></p>
<p>In the report, Hamilton felt that the debt that the United States had accrued during the Revolutionary War was the price it paid for its liberty. He argued that liberty and property security were inseparable and that the government should honor the contracts, as they formed the basis of public and private morality. To Hamilton, the proper handling of the government debt would also allow America to borrow at affordable interest rates and would also be a stimulant to the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-36"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:297</sup></p>
<p>Hamilton divided the debt into national and state, and further divided the national debt into foreign and domestic debt. While there was agreement on how to handle the foreign debt (especially with France), there was not with regards to the national debt held by domestic creditors. During the Revolutionary War, affluent citizens had invested in bonds, and war veterans had been paid with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Promissory_notes" title="Promissory notes">promissory notes</a> and <a href="/wiki/IOU" title="IOU">IOUs</a> that plummeted in price during the Confederation. In response, the war veterans sold the securities to speculators for as little as fifteen to twenty cents on the dollar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-37"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:297–98</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton felt the money from the bonds should not go to the soldiers, but the speculators that had bought the bonds from the soldiers, as they had little faith in the country's future. The process of attempting to track down the original bond holders along with the government showing discrimination among the classes of holders if the war veterans were to be compensated also weighed in as factors for Hamilton. As for the state debts, Hamilton suggested to consolidate it with the national debt and label it as federal debt, for the sake of efficiency on a national scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-38"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:298–99</sup></p>
<p>The last portion of the report dealt with eliminating the debt by utilizing a <a href="/wiki/Sinking_fund" title="Sinking fund">sinking fund</a> that would retire five percent of the debt annually until it was paid off. Due to the bonds being traded well below their face value, the purchases would benefit the government as the securities rose in price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-39"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:300</sup></p>
<p>When the report was submitted to the House of Representatives, detractors soon began to speak against it. The notion of programs that resembled British practice were wicked along with the power of balance being shifted away from the Representatives to the executive branch were some of the prejudices that resided within the House. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Maclay_(politician)" title="William Maclay (politician)">William Maclay</a> suspected that several congressmen were involved in government securities, saw Congress in an unholy league with New York speculators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-40"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:302</sup> Congressman <a href="/wiki/James_Jackson_(Georgia_politician)" title="James Jackson (Georgia politician)">James Jackson</a> also spoke against New York, with allegations of speculators attempting to swindle those who had yet heard about Hamilton's report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-41"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:303</sup></p>
