<p>The involvement of those in Hamilton's circle such as Schuyler, William Duer, James Duane, Gouverneur Morris, and <a href="/wiki/Rufus_King" title="Rufus King">Rufus King</a> as speculators was not favorable to those against the report, either, though Hamilton personally did not own or deal a share in the debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-42"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:304</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-6"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:250</sup> Madison eventually spoke against it by February 1790. Although he was not against current holders of government debt to profit, he wanted the windfall to go to the original holders. Madison did not feel that the original holders had lost faith in the government, but sold their securities out of desperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-43"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:305</sup> The compromise was seen as egregious to both Hamiltonians and their dissidents such as Maclay, and Madison's vote was defeated 36 votes to 13 on February 22.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-44"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:305</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-7"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:255</sup></p>
<p>The fight for the national government to assume state debt was a longer issue, and lasted over four months. During the period, the resources that Hamilton was to apply to the payment of state debts was requested by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_White_(Virginia)" title="Alexander White (Virginia)">Alexander White</a>, and was rejected due to Hamilton's not being able to prepare information by March 3, and was even postponed by his own supporters in spite of configuring a report the next day (which consisted of a series of additional duties to meet the interest on the state debts).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-8"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:297–98</sup></p>
<p>Some of the other issues involving Hamilton were bypassing the rising issue of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a> in Congress after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker">Quakers</a> petitioned for its abolition (though he returned to the issue the following year),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-45"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:307</sup> having Duer resign as Assistant Secretary of the Treasury, and the vote of assumption being voted down 31 votes to 29 on April 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-9"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:258–59</sup></p>
<p>The temporary location of the capital from New York City also played a role, as <a href="/wiki/Tench_Coxe" title="Tench Coxe">Tench Coxe</a> was sent to speak to Maclay to bargain about the capital being temporarily located to Philadelphia, as a single vote in the Senate was needed and five in the House for the bill to pass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-10"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:263</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[note 2]</a></sup> The bill passed in the Senate on July 21 and in the House 34 votes to 28 on July 26, 1790.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-11"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:263</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Report_on_a_National_Bank">Report on a National Bank</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton's Report on a National Bank was a projection from the first Report on the Public Credit. Although Hamilton had been forming ideas of a national bank as early as 1779,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-12"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:268</sup> he gathered ideas in various ways over the past eleven years. These included theories from Adam Smith,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> extensive studies on the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, the blunders of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_North_America" title="Bank of North America">Bank of North America</a> and his experience in establishing the Bank of New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke82_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke82-74">[72]</a></sup> He also used American records from <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson" title="James Wilson">James Wilson</a>, Pelatiah Webster, <a href="/wiki/Gouverneur_Morris" title="Gouverneur Morris">Gouverneur Morris</a>, and from his assistant Treasury secretary Tench Coxe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke82_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke82-74">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton suggested that Congress should charter the <a href="/wiki/First_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="First Bank of the United States">National Bank</a> with a capitalization of $10 million, one-fifth of which would be handled by the Government. Since the Government did not have the money, it would borrow the money from the bank itself, and repay the loan in ten even annual installments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:194</sup> The rest was to be available to individual investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> The bank was to be governed by a twenty-five member board of directors that was to represent a large majority of the private shareholders, which Hamilton considered essential for his being under a private direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-13"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:268</sup> Hamilton's bank model had many similarities to that of the Bank of England, except Hamilton wanted to exclude the Government from being involved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_debt" title="Public debt">public debt</a>, but provide a large, firm, and elastic money supply for the functioning of normal businesses and usual economic development, among other differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:194–95</sup> For tax revenue to ignite the bank, it was the same as he had previously proposed; increases on imported spirits: rum, liquor, and whiskey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:195–96</sup></p>
<p>The bill passed through the Senate practically without a problem, but objections of the proposal increased by the time it reached the House of Representatives. It was generally held by critics that Hamilton was serving the interests of the Northeast by means of the bank,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> and those of the agrarian lifestyle would not benefit from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-14"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:270</sup> Among those critics was James Jackson of Georgia, who also attempted to refute the report by quoting from <i>The Federalist</i> Papers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-15"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:270</sup> Madison and Jefferson also opposed the bank bill; however, the potential of the capital not being moved to the Potomac if the bank was to have a firm establishment in Philadelphia (the current capital of the United States) was a more significant reason, and actions that Pennsylvania members of Congress took to keep the capital there made both men anxious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:199–200</sup> Madison warned the Pennsylvania congress members that he would attack the bill as unconstitutional in the House, and followed up on his threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:200</sup> Madison argued his case of where the power of a bank could be established within the Constitution, but he failed to sway members of the House, and his authority on the constitution was questioned by a few members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:200–01</sup> The bill eventually passed in an overwhelming fashion 39 to 20, on February 8, 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-16"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:271</sup></p>
<p>Washington hesitated to sign the bill, as he received suggestions from Attorney-General <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Randolph" title="Edmund Randolph">Edmund Randolph</a> and Thomas Jefferson. Jefferson dismissed the 'necessary and proper' clause as reasoning for the creation of a national bank, stating that the enumerated powers "can all be carried into execution without a bank." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-17"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:271–72</sup> Along with Randolph and Jefferson's objections, Washington's involvement in the movement of the capital from Philadelphia is also thought to be a reason for his hesitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-8"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:202–03</sup> In response to the objection of the 'necessary and proper' clause, Hamilton stated that "Necessary often means no more than needful, requisite, incidental, useful, or conductive to", and the bank was a "convenient species of medium in which they (taxes) are to be paid." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-18"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:272–73</sup> Washington would eventually sign the bill into law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-19"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:272–73</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_the_U.S._Mint">Establishing the U.S. Mint</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="1638" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg/220px-1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg/330px-1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg/440px-1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1795_eagle_obverse_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Turban_Head_eagle" title="Turban Head eagle">Turban Head eagle</a>, one of the first gold mints following the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act of 1792</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1791, Hamilton submitted Report on the Establishment of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" title="Mint (coin)">Mint</a> to the House of Representatives. Most of Hamilton's ideas for this report were from European economists, resolutions from Continental Congress meetings from 1785 and 1786, and from people such as Gouverneur Morris and Thomas Jefferson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-9"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:197</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">Spanish coin</a> being the most circulated coin in the United States at the time, Alexander Hamilton proposed that the minting of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">United States dollar</a> weighing almost as much as the Spanish peso would be the simplest way to introduce a national currency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Hamilton wanted the U.S. dollar system to be set for decimals rather than the eights like the Spanish mint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> In spite of preferring a monometallic <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Studentski_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Studentski-80">[78]</a></sup> he issued a <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard#Bimetallic_standard" title="Gold standard">bimetallic currency</a> at ratio that was to be similar to most European countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-10"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:197</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> What was different from the European currencies was his desire to overprice the gold on the grounds that the United States would always receive an influx of silver from the West Indies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-11"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:197</sup> Hamilton desired the minting of small value coins such as silver ten-cent and copper cent and half-cent pieces, for reducing the cost of living for the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-12"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:198</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke82_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke82-74">[72]</a></sup> One of his main objectives was for the general public to become accustomed to handling money on a frequent basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-13"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:198</sup></p>
<p>By 1792, Hamilton's principles were adopted by Congress, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Coinage_Act_of_1792" title="Coinage Act of 1792">Coinage Act of 1792</a>, and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a>. There was to be a ten dollar Gold Eagle coin, a silver dollar, and fractional money ranging from one-half to fifty cents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Studentski_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Studentski-80">[78]</a></sup> The coining of silver and gold was issued by 1795.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Studentski_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-Studentski-80">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revenue_Cutter_Service">Revenue Cutter Service</span></h3>
<p>Smuggling off American coasts was an issue before the Revolutionary War, and after the Revolution it was more problematic. Along with smuggling, lack of shipping control, pirating, and a revenue unbalance were also major problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibowicz07_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibowicz07-82">[80]</a></sup> In response, Hamilton proposed to Congress to enact a naval police force called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revenue_Cutter_Service" title="Revenue Cutter Service">revenue cutters</a> in order to patrol the waters and assist the custom collectors with confiscating contraband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-46"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:340</sup> This idea was also proposed to assist in tariff controlling, boosting the American economy, and promote the merchant marine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibowicz07_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibowicz07-82">[80]</a></sup> It is thought that his experience obtained during his apprenticeship with Nicholas Kruger was influential in his decision-making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-47"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:32</sup></p>
<p>Concerning some of the details of the "System of Cutters",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[note 3]</a></sup> Hamilton wanted the first ten <a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">cutters</a> in different areas in the United States, from New England to Georgia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-48"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:340</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> Each of those cutters was to be armed with ten muskets and bayonets, twenty pistols, two chisels, one broad-ax and two lanterns; the fabric of the sails was to be domestically manufactured;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-49"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:340</sup> and provisions were made for the employees' food supply and etiquette when boarding ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-50"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:340</sup> Congress established the Revenue Cutter Service on August 4, 1790, which is viewed as the birth of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibowicz07_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gibowicz07-82">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whiskey_as_tax_revenue">Whiskey as tax revenue</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion" title="Whiskey Rebellion">Whiskey Rebellion</a></div>
<p>One of the principal sources of revenue Hamilton prevailed upon Congress to approve was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excise_tax" title="Excise tax">excise tax</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiskey" title="Whiskey">whiskey</a>. In his first Tariff Bill in January 1790, Hamilton proposed to raise the three million dollars needed to pay for government operating expenses and interest on domestic and foreign debts by means of an increase on duties on imported wines, distilled spirits, tea, coffee, and domestic spirits. It failed, with Congress complying with most recommendations excluding the excise tax on Whiskey (Madison's tariff of the same year was a modification of Hamilton's that involved only imported duties and was passed in September).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response of diversifying revenues, as three-fourths of revenue gathered was from commerce with Great Britain, Hamilton attempted once again during his <i>Report on Public Credit</i> when presenting it in 1790 to implement an excise tax both imported and domestic spirits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-51"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:342</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray141_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray141-87">[84]</a></sup> The taxation rate was graduated in proportion to the whiskey proof, and Hamilton intended to equalize the tax burden on imported spirits with imported and domestic liquor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray141_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray141-87">[84]</a></sup> In lieu of the excise on production citizens could pay 60 cents by the gallon of dispensing capacity, along with an exemption on small stills used exclusively for domestic consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray141_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Murray141-87">[84]</a></sup> He realized the loathing that the tax would receive in rural areas, but thought of the taxing of spirits more reasonable than land taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-52"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:342</sup></p>
<p>Opposition initially came from Pennsylvania's House of Representatives protesting the tax. William Maclay had noted that not even the Pennsylvanian legislators had been able to enforce excise taxes in the western regions of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-53"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:342–43</sup> Hamilton was aware of the potential difficulties and proposed inspectors the ability to search buildings that distillers were designated to store their spirits, and would be able to search suspected illegal storage facilities to confiscate contraband with a warrant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray40_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray40-88">[85]</a></sup> Although the inspectors were not allowed to search houses and warehouses, they were to visit twice a day and file weekly reports in extensive detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-54"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:343</sup> Hamilton cautioned against expedited judicial means, and favored a jury trial with potential offenders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray40_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray40-88">[85]</a></sup> As soon as 1791 locals began to shun or threaten inspectors, as they felt the inspection methods were intrusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-55"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:343</sup> Inspectors were also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tarred_and_feathered" title="Tarred and feathered">tarred and feathered</a>, blindfolded, and whipped. Hamilton had attempted to appease the opposition with lowered tax rates, but it did not suffice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-56"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:468</sup></p>
<p>Strong opposition to the whiskey tax by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cottage_industry" title="Cottage industry">cottage producers</a> in remote, rural regions erupted into the <a href="/wiki/Whiskey_Rebellion" title="Whiskey Rebellion">Whiskey Rebellion</a> in 1794; in <a href="/wiki/Western_Pennsylvania" title="Western Pennsylvania">Western Pennsylvania</a> and western <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, whiskey was the basic export product and was fundamental to the local economy. In response to the rebellion, believing compliance with the laws was vital to the establishment of federal authority, Hamilton accompanied to the rebellion's site President Washington, General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry "Light Horse Harry" Lee</a>, and more federal troops than were ever assembled in one place during the Revolution. This overwhelming display of force intimidated the leaders of the insurrection, ending the rebellion virtually without bloodshed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing_and_industry">Manufacturing and industry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="600" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg/220px-DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg/330px-DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg/440px-DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DSCN2897_hamilton_statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Hamilton by <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Simmons" title="Franklin Simmons">Franklin Simmons</a>, overlooking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Falls_of_the_Passaic_River" title="Great Falls of the Passaic River">Great Falls of the Passaic River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paterson,_New_Jersey" title="Paterson, New Jersey">Paterson</a>, New Jersey, where Hamilton envisioned using the falls to power new factories</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hamilton's next report was his <i>Report on Manufactures</i>. Although he was requested by Congress on January 15, 1790 for a report for manufacturing that would expand the United States' independence, the report was not submitted until December 5, 1791.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-20"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:274, 277</sup> In the report, Hamilton quoted from <i>Wealth of Nations</i> and used the French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiocrats" title="Physiocrats">physiocrats</a> as an example for rejecting <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarianism</a> and the physiocratic theory; respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-14"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:233</sup> Hamilton also refuted Smith's ideas of government noninterference, as it would have been detrimental for trade with other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-15"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:244</sup> Hamilton also thought of the United States being a primarily agrarian country would be a disadvantage in dealing with Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> In response to the agrarian detractors, Hamilton stated that the agriculturists' interest would be advanced by manufactures,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-21"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:276</sup> and that agriculture was just as productive as manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-16"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:233</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-22"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:276</sup></p>
<p>Among the ways that the government could assist in manufacturing, Hamilton mentioned levying protective duties on imported foreign goods that were also manufactured in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke101_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke101-91">[88]</a></sup> to withdraw duties levied on raw materials needed for domestic manufacturing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-23"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:277</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke101_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke101-91">[88]</a></sup> pecuniary boundaries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-24"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:277</sup> and encouraging immigration for people to better themselves in similar employment opportunities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke101_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke101-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup> Congress shelved the report without much debate (except for Madison's objection to Hamilton's formulation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Welfare_clause" title="General Welfare clause">General Welfare clause</a>, which Hamilton construed liberally as a legal basis for his extensive programs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Subsequently, in 1791, with his ideas for manufacturing being a major influence, Hamilton, along with Coxe and several entrepreneurs from New York and Philadelphia helped form the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_for_the_Establishment_of_Useful_Manufactures" title="Society for the Establishment of Useful Manufactures">Society for the Establishment of Useful Manufactures</a>, a private industrial corporation. The location at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Falls_of_the_Passaic_River" title="Great Falls of the Passaic River">Great Falls of the Passaic River</a> in New Jersey was selected due to access to raw materials, it being densely inhabited, and having access to water power from the falls of the Passaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-17"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:231</sup> The factory town was named Paterson after New Jersey's Governor <a href="/wiki/William_Paterson_(judge)" title="William Paterson (judge)">William Paterson</a>, who signed the charter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-18"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:232</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke103_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke103-94">[91]</a></sup> The profits were to derive from specific corporates rather than the benefits to be conferred to the nation and the citizens, which was unlike the report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke102_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke102-95">[92]</a></sup> Hamilton also suggested the first stock to be offered at $500,000 and to eventually increase to $1 million, and welcomed state and national government subscriptions alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-25"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:280</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke102_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke102-95">[92]</a></sup> The company was never successful: numerous shareholders reneged on stock payments, some members soon went bankrupt, and <a href="/wiki/William_Duer_(Continental_Congressman)" title="William Duer (Continental Congressman)">William Duer</a>, the governor of the program, was sent to debtors' prison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-26"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:280</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke103_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke103-94">[91]</a></sup> In spite of Hamilton's efforts to mend the disaster, the company would expire by 1796.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke103_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke103-94">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_parties">Emergence of parties</span></h3>
<p>During Hamilton's tenure as Treasury Secretary, political factions began to emerge. A Congressional caucus, led by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Branch_Giles" title="William Branch Giles">William Branch Giles</a>, began as an opposition group to Hamilton's financial programs, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> joined this group when he returned from France. Hamilton and his allies began to call themselves <i><a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalists</a></i>. The opposition group, now called the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> by political scientists, was at the time known as <i>Republicans</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[note 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton assembled a nationwide coalition to garner support for the Administration, including the expansive financial programs Hamilton had made Administration policy and especially the president's policy of neutrality in the European war between Britain and France. Hamilton's public relations campaign attacked the French minister <a href="/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt" title="Edmond-Charles Genêt">Edmond-Charles Genêt</a> (he called himself "Citizen Genêt") who tried to appeal to voters directly, which Federalists denounced as foreign interference in American affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup> If Hamilton's administrative republic was to succeed, Americans had to see themselves as nation citizens, and experience an administration that proved firm and demonstrated the concepts found within the United States Constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cook2014_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cook2014-99">[95]</a></sup> The Federalists did impose some internal direct taxes but they departed from the most implications of the Hamilton administrative republic as risky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Jeffersonian Republicans opposed banks and cities, and favored France. They built their own national coalition to oppose the Federalists. Both sides gained the support of local political factions; each side developed its own partisan newspapers. <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Fenno" title="John Fenno">John Fenno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Cobbett" title="William Cobbett">William Cobbett</a> were energetic editors for the Federalists; <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Bache_(journalist)" title="Benjamin Franklin Bache (journalist)">Benjamin Franklin Bache</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Freneau" title="Philip Freneau">Philip Freneau</a> were fiery Republican editors. All the newspapers were characterized by intense personal attacks, major exaggerations and invented claims. In 1801, Hamilton established a daily newspaper, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Evening_Post" title="New York Evening Post">New York Evening Post</a></i> and brought in <a href="/wiki/William_Coleman_(editor)" title="William Coleman (editor)">William Coleman</a> as editor. It is still publishing (as the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Post" title="New York Post">New York Post</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>The quarrel between Hamilton and Jefferson is the best known and historically the most important in American political history. Hamilton's and Jefferson's incompatibility was heightened by the unavowed wish of each to be Washington's principal and most trusted advisor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jay_Treaty_and_Britain">Jay Treaty and Britain</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jay%27s-treaty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="546" data-file-width="334" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jay%27s-treaty.jpg/180px-Jay%27s-treaty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Jay%27s-treaty.jpg/270px-Jay%27s-treaty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Jay%27s-treaty.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jay%27s-treaty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Jay Treaty</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Coalition" title="First Coalition">France and Britain went to war</a> in early 1793, all four members of the Cabinet were consulted on what to do. They and Washington unanimously agreed to remain neutral, and to send Genêt home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elkins_McK_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elkins_McK-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:336–41</sup> However, in 1794 policy toward Britain became a major point of contention between the two parties. Hamilton and the Federalists wished for more trade with Britain, the new nation's largest trading partner. The Republicans saw Britain as the main threat to republicanism and proposed instead a trade war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-27"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:327–28</sup></p>
<p>To avoid war, Washington sent Chief Justice John Jay to negotiate with the British; Hamilton largely wrote Jay's instructions. The result was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jay%27s_Treaty" title="Jay's Treaty">Jay's Treaty</a>. It was denounced by the Republicans, but Hamilton mobilized support throughout the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup> The Jay Treaty passed the Senate in 1795 by exactly the required two-thirds majority. The Treaty resolved issues remaining from the Revolution, averted war, and made possible ten years of peaceful trade between the United States and Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elkins_McK_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elkins_McK-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:Ch 9</sup> Historian George Herring notes the "remarkable and fortuitous economic and diplomatic gains" produced by the Treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several European nations had formed a <a href="/wiki/Second_League_of_Armed_Neutrality" title="Second League of Armed Neutrality">League of Armed Neutrality</a> against incursions on their neutral rights; the Cabinet was also consulted on whether the United States should join it, and decided not to. It kept that decision secret, but Hamilton revealed it in private to George Hammond, the British Minister to the United States, without telling Jay or anyone else. (His act remained unknown until Hammond's dispatches were read in the 1920s). This "amazing revelation" may have had limited effect on the negotiations; Jay did threaten to join the League at one point, but the British had other reasons not to view the League as a serious threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elkins_McK_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Elkins_McK-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:411 ff</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Report_on_Public_Credit">Second Report on Public Credit</span></h3>
<p>Before leaving his post in 1795, Hamilton submitted <i>Report on a Plan for the Further Support of Public Credit</i> to Congress to curb the debt problem. Hamilton grew dissatisfied with what he viewed as a lack of a comprehensive plan to fix the public debt. He wished to have new taxes passed with older ones made permanent and stated that any surplus from the excise tax on liquor would be pledged to lower public debt. His proposals were included into a bill by Congress within slightly over a month after his departure as treasury secretary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-57"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:480</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Secretary_years">Post-Secretary years</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Reynolds_affair">The Reynolds affair</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hamilton%E2%80%93Reynolds_sex_scandal" title="Hamilton–Reynolds sex scandal">Hamilton–Reynolds sex scandal</a></div>
<p>Hamilton is the first major American politician known to have had a <a href="/wiki/Sex_scandal" title="Sex scandal">sex scandal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> In the summer of 1791, Hamilton became involved in an affair with <a href="/wiki/Maria_Reynolds" title="Maria Reynolds">Maria Reynolds</a> over approximately a nine-month period. According to Hamilton's recount, Reynolds appeared to Hamilton as a woman who had been abandoned by her husband, James, at New York and wished to return there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-28"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:366–69</sup> Hamilton did not have any money on his person, so he retrieved her address in order to deliver the funds in person. Once Hamilton arrived at the boarding house where Maria was lodging, she brought him upstairs and led him into her bedroom where he recounts that "Some conversation ensued from which it was quickly apparent that other than pecuniary consolation would be acceptable”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_Pamphlet_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds_Pamphlet-108">[104]</a></sup> After this he had frequent meetings with her, most of them in his own house, his wife Elizabeth being absent with their children on a summer holiday in <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Albany" title="Albany">Albany</a> visiting Elizabeth's parents. The affair continued in the Fall, though with decreasing frequency and after Hamilton had showed unequivocal signs that he wanted to end the affair<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Draft_of_the_Reynolds_Pamphlet_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Draft_of_the_Reynolds_Pamphlet-109">[105]</a></sup> Hamilton received two letters on December 15, 1791, one each from Mr. and Mrs. Reynolds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-29"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:366–69</sup> The first letter, from Maria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> warned of her husband's knowledge and of James' attempting to <a href="/wiki/Blackmail" title="Blackmail">blackmail</a> Hamilton. By this point Hamilton discontinued the affair and briefly ceased to visit, but both Reynoldses apparently were involved in the blackmailing scheme as both sent letters inviting Hamilton to continue his visits;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-30"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:366–69</sup> after extorting $1000 in exchange for secrecy over Hamilton's adultery, James Reynolds rethought his request that Hamilton cease his relationship with Maria and wrote inviting him to renew his visits "as a friend",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup> only to extort forced "loans" after each visit. By 2 May 1792, James Reynolds changed his mind again and requested that Hamilton no longer see his wife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup> but not before receiving additional payment; in the end the blackmail payments totaled over $1300 including the initial extortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-31"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:369</sup> Hamilton at this point was possibly aware of both Reynoldses' being involved in the blackmail<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup> and welcomed as well as strictly complied with Reynolds' request to end the affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_Pamphlet_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds_Pamphlet-108">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1792 James Reynolds and his associate Jacob Clingman were arrested for counterfeiting and speculating in veteran back wages. Clingman was released on bail and relayed information to <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> that Reynolds had evidence that would incriminate Hamilton. Monroe consulted with Congressmen Muhlenberg and Venable on what actions to take and the Congressmen confronted Hamilton on December 15, 1792.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reynolds_Pamphlet_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds_Pamphlet-108">[104]</a></sup> Hamilton refuted the suspicions of speculation by exposing his affair with Maria and producing as evidence the letters by both Reynoldses, proving that his payments to James Reynolds related to blackmail over his adultery, and not to treasury misconduct. The trio were to keep the documents privately with the utmost confidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-32"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:366–69</sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 1797, however, when "notoriously scurrilous journalist" <a href="/wiki/James_T._Callender" title="James T. Callender">James T. Callender</a> published <i>A History of the United States for the Year 1796</i>, it contained accusations of James Reynolds being an agent of Hamilton, using documents from the confrontation of December 15, 1792. On July 5, 1797, Hamilton wrote to Monroe, Muhlenberg and Venable asking them to confirm that there was nothing that would damage the perception of his integrity while Secretary of Treasury. All complied with Hamilton's request but Monroe ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-58"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup> and after several rounds of argument, the two almost resorted to a duel. When Hamilton did not obtain an explicit response from Monroe, he published a 100-page booklet, later usually referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reynolds_Pamphlet" title="Reynolds Pamphlet">Reynolds Pamphlet</a>, in order to preserve his public reputation, and discussed the affair in exquisite detail. His wife forgave him, but not Monroe. Though he faced ridicule from the Democratic-Republican faction, he maintained his availability for public service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-19"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:334–36</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1796_presidential_election">1796 presidential election</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton's resignation as Secretary of the Treasury in 1795 did not remove him from public life. With the resumption of his law practice, he remained close to Washington as an advisor and friend. Hamilton influenced Washington in the composition of his <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s_Farewell_Address" title="George Washington's Farewell Address">Farewell Address</a> by writing drafts for Washington to compare with the latter's draft, although when Washington contemplated retirement in 1792, he had consulted James Madison for a draft that was used in a similar manner to Hamilton's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the election of 1796, under the Constitution as it stood then, each of the presidential <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">electors</a> had two votes, which they were to cast for different men. The one who received most votes would become President, the second-most, Vice President. This system was not designed with the operation of parties in mind, as they had been thought disreputable and factious. The Federalists planned to deal with this by having all their Electors vote for <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, the Vice President, and all but a few for <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pinckney" title="Thomas Pinckney">Thomas Pinckney</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-59"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:117</sup></p>
<p>Adams resented Hamilton's influence with Washington and considered him overambitious and scandalous in his private life; Hamilton compared Adams unfavorably with Washington and thought him too emotionally unstable to be President.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-60"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:510</sup> Hamilton took the election as an opportunity: he urged all the northern electors to vote for Adams and Pinckney, lest Jefferson get in; but he cooperated with <a href="/wiki/Edward_Rutledge" title="Edward Rutledge">Edward Rutledge</a> to have South Carolina's electors vote for Jefferson and Pinckney. If all this worked, Pinckney would have more votes than Adams, Pinckney would become President, and Adams would remain Vice President, but it did not work. The Federalists found out about it (even the French minister to the United States knew), and northern Federalists voted for Adams but <i>not</i> for Pinckney, in sufficient numbers that Pinckney came in third and Jefferson became Vice President.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup> Adams resented the intrigue since he felt his service to the nation was much more extensive than Pinckney's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_By_William_J_Weaver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="251" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Alexander_Hamilton_By_William_J_Weaver.jpg/220px-Alexander_Hamilton_By_William_J_Weaver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Alexander_Hamilton_By_William_J_Weaver.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_By_William_J_Weaver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alexander Hamilton by <a href="/wiki/William_J._Weaver" title="William J. Weaver">William J. Weaver</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the military build-up of the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> of 1798–1800, and with the strong endorsement of Washington (who had been called out of retirement to lead the Army if a French invasion materialized), Adams reluctantly appointed Hamilton a <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">major general</a> of the army. At Washington's insistence, Hamilton was made the senior major general, prompting <a href="/wiki/Henry_Knox" title="Henry Knox">Henry Knox</a> to decline appointment to serve as Hamilton's junior (Knox had been a major general in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> and thought it would be degrading to serve beneath him).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-61"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:558–60</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup> Hamilton served as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Inspectors_General_of_the_U.S._Army" title="List of Inspectors General of the U.S. Army">inspector general of the United States Army</a> from July 18, 1798, to June 15, 1800; because Washington was unwilling to leave Mount Vernon unless it were to command an army in the field, Hamilton was the <i>de facto</i> head of the army, to Adams's considerable displeasure. If full-scale war broke out with France, Hamilton argued that the army should conquer the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_North_America" title="Spanish North America">North American colonies</a> of France's ally, Spain, bordering the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup> Hamilton was prepared to march his army through the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a> if necessary, possibly also using his army in Virginia to quash opposition to Adams and himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>To fund this army, Hamilton wrote regularly to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oliver_Wolcott,_Jr." title="Oliver Wolcott, Jr.">Oliver Wolcott, Jr.</a>, his successor at the Treasury; <a href="/wiki/William_Loughton_Smith" title="William Loughton Smith">William Loughton Smith</a>, of the House Ways and Means Committee; and Senator <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Sedgwick" title="Theodore Sedgwick">Theodore Sedgwick</a> of Massachusetts. He directed them to pass a direct tax to fund the war. Smith resigned in July 1797, as Hamilton scolded him for slowness, and told Wolcott to tax houses instead of land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup> The eventual program included a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stamp_Act" title="Stamp Act">Stamp Act</a> like that of the British before the Revolution and other taxes on land, houses, and slaves, calculated at different rates in different states, and requiring difficult and intricate assessment of houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup> This provoked resistance in southeastern Pennsylvania, led primarily by men such as <a href="/wiki/John_Fries" title="John Fries">John Fries</a> who had marched with Washington against the Whiskey Rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton aided in all areas of the army's development, and after Washington's death he was by default the <a href="/wiki/Commanding_General_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Commanding General of the United States Army">Senior Officer of the United States Army</a> from December 14, 1799, to June 15, 1800. The army was to guard against invasion from France. Adams, however, derailed all plans for war by opening negotiations with France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup> Adams had held it proper to retain the members of Washington's cabinet, except for cause; he found, in 1800 (after Washington's death), that they were obeying Hamilton rather than himself, and fired several of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1800_presidential_election">1800 presidential election</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1352" data-file-width="912" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg/170px-AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg/255px-AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg/340px-AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderHamiltonUSCapStat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Hamilton in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_rotunda" title="United States Capitol rotunda">United States Capitol rotunda</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>
<p>In the 1800 election, Hamilton worked to defeat not only the rival Democratic-Republican candidates, but also his party's own nominee, John Adams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-33"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:392–99</sup> In November 1799, the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a> had left one Democratic-Republican newspaper functioning in New York City; when the last, the <i>New Daily Advertiser</i>, reprinted an article saying that Hamilton had attempted to purchase the Philadelphia <i>Aurora</i> and close it down, Hamilton had the publisher prosecuted for <a href="/wiki/Seditious_libel" title="Seditious libel">seditious libel</a>, and the prosecution compelled the owner to close the paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aaron Burr had won New York for Jefferson in May; now Hamilton proposed a rerun of the election under different rules—with carefully drawn districts and each choosing an elector—such that the Federalists would split the electoral vote of New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[note 5]</a></sup> (John Jay, a Federalist who had given up the Supreme Court to be Governor of New York, wrote on the back of the letter the words, "Proposing a measure for party purposes which it would not become me to adopt," and declined to reply.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>John Adams was running this time with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a> of South Carolina (the elder brother of candidate Thomas Pinckney from the 1796 election). Hamilton now toured <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, again urging northern electors to hold firm for Pinckney in the renewed hope of making Pinckney president; and he again intrigued in South Carolina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-20"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:350–51</sup> Hamilton's ideas involved coaxing middle-state Federalists to assert their non-support for Adams if there was no support for Pinckney and writing to more of the modest supports of Adams concerning his supposed misconduct while president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-21"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:350–51</sup> Hamilton expected to see southern states such as the Carolinas cast their votes for Pinckney and Jefferson, and would result in the former being ahead of both Adams and Jefferson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-34"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:394–95</sup></p>
<p>In accordance with the second of the aforementioned plans, and a recent personal rift with Adams,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-22"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:351</sup> Hamilton wrote a pamphlet called <i>Letter from Alexander Hamilton, Concerning the Public Conduct and Character of John Adams, Esq. President of the United States</i> that was highly critical of him, though it closed with a tepid endorsement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-35"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:396</sup> He mailed this to two hundred leading Federalists; when a copy fell into the Democratic-Republicans' hands, they printed it. This hurt Adams's 1800 reelection campaign and split the Federalist Party, virtually assuring the victory of the Democratic-Republican Party, led by Jefferson, in the election of 1800; it destroyed Hamilton's position among the Federalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jefferson had beaten Adams, but both he and his running mate, Aaron Burr, had received 73 votes in the Electoral College (Adams finished in third place, Pinckney in fourth, and Jay received one vote). With Jefferson and Burr tied, the United States House of Representatives had to choose between the two men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-23"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:352</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-36"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:399</sup> Several Federalists who opposed Jefferson supported Burr, and for the first 35 ballots, Jefferson was denied a majority. Before the 36th ballot, Hamilton threw his weight behind Jefferson, supporting the arrangement reached by <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James A. Bayard</a> of Delaware, in which five Federalist Representatives from Maryland and Vermont abstained from voting, allowing those states' delegations to go for Jefferson, ending the impasse and electing Jefferson President rather than Burr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-24"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:350–51</sup> Even though Hamilton did not like Jefferson and disagreed with him on many issues, he viewed Jefferson as the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_of_two_evils_principle" title="Lesser of two evils principle">lesser of two evils</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Hamilton spoke of Jefferson as being "by far not so a dangerous man", and that Burr was a "mischievous enemy" to the principle measure of the past administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup> There is strong circumstantial evidence,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (October 2016)">weasel words</span></a></i>]</sup> however, that what Hamilton really feared was Burr's appeal to the members of the Federalist Party and loss of his control over them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Alexander_Hamilton#Dubious" title="Talk:Alexander Hamilton">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (October 2016)">weasel words</span></a></i>]</sup> Federalists viewed Burr as a moderate who was willing to dialogue with them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (October 2016)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Opinion" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:ASF#A_simple_formulation" title="Wikipedia:ASF"><span title="This is opinion presented as fact (October 2016)">opinion</span></a></i>]</sup> It was for that reason, along with the fact that Burr was a northerner and not a Virginian, that many Federalist Representatives voted for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup> Hamilton wrote an exceeding number of letters to friends in Congress to convince the members to see otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-25"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:352</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-37"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:401</sup> However, the Federalists rejected Hamilton's diatribe as reasons to not vote for Burr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDonald_24-26"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:353</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-38"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:401</sup> Nevertheless, Burr would become Vice President of the United States. When it became clear that Jefferson had developed his own concerns about Burr and would not support his return to the Vice Presidency,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Burr sought the New York governorship in 1804 with Federalist support, against the Jeffersonian <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Lewis_(governor)" title="Morgan Lewis (governor)">Morgan Lewis</a>, but was defeated by forces including Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Duel_with_Burr_and_death">Duel with Burr and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="442" data-file-width="640" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/220px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/330px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg/440px-Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton-burr-duel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Hamilton-Burr Duel</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG/170px-Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG/255px-Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG/340px-Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_Grave.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamilton's tomb in the graveyard of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_Church,_New_York" title="Trinity Church, New York">Trinity Church</a> at <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">Broadway</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">Burr–Hamilton duel</a></div>
<p>Soon after the 1804 gubernatorial election in New York—in which <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Lewis_(governor)" title="Morgan Lewis (governor)">Morgan Lewis</a>, greatly assisted by Hamilton, defeated <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>—the <i>Albany Register</i> published <a href="/wiki/Charles_D._Cooper" title="Charles D. Cooper">Charles D. Cooper</a>'s letters, citing Hamilton's opposition to Burr and alleging that Hamilton had expressed "a still more despicable opinion" of the Vice President at an upstate New York dinner party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jbf_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-jbf-133">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup> Cooper claimed that the letter was intercepted after relaying the information, but stated he was 'unusually cautious' in recollecting the information from the dinner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-62"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:680–81</sup> Burr, sensing an attack on his honor, and recovering from his defeat, demanded an apology in letter form. Hamilton wrote a letter in response and ultimately refused because he could not recall the instance of insulting Burr; also, Hamilton would have been accused of recanting Cooper's letter out of cowardice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-39"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:423–24</sup> After a series of attempts to reconcile were to no avail, a duel was arranged through liaisons on June 27, 1804.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-40"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:426</sup></p>
<p>The night before the duel, Hamilton wrote a defense of his decision to duel. Hamilton viewed his roles of being a father and husband, putting his creditors at risk, placing his family's welfare in jeopardy and his moral and religious stances as reasons not to duel, but he felt it impossible to avoid due to having made attacks on Burr which he was unable to recant, and because of Burr's behavior prior to the duel. He attempted to reconcile his moral and religious reasons and the codes of honor and politics. He intended to accept the duel and throw his fire in order to satisfy his morals and political codes, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-63"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:689</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jbf_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-jbf-133">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[note 6]</a></sup> His desire to be available for future political matters also played a factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jbf_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-jbf-133">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The duel began at dawn on July 11, 1804, along the west bank of the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> on a rocky ledge in <a href="/wiki/Weehawken,_New_Jersey" title="Weehawken, New Jersey">Weehawken</a>, New Jersey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Duel#Offense_and_satisfaction" title="Duel">seconds</a> measured the paces, Hamilton, according to both <a href="/wiki/William_P._Van_Ness" title="William P. Van Ness">William P. Van Ness</a> and Burr, raised his pistol "as if to try the light" and had to wear his spectacles to prevent his vision from being obscured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup> Hamilton also refused the hairspring set of dueling pistols (that would make the pulling of the trigger lighter) offered by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Pendleton" title="Nathaniel Pendleton">Nathaniel Pendleton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookhiser15_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brookhiser15-138">[132]</a></sup> Vice President Burr shot Hamilton, delivering what proved to be a fatal wound. Hamilton's shot broke a tree branch directly above Burr's head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-64"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:117</sup> Neither of the seconds, Pendleton nor Van Ness, could determine who fired first,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup> as each claimed that the other man had fired first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brookhiser15_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brookhiser15-138">[132]</a></sup> Soon after, they measured and triangulated the shooting, but could not determine from which angle Hamilton fired. Burr's shot, however, hit Hamilton in the lower abdomen above the right hip. The bullet ricocheted off Hamilton's second or third <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_ribs" title="False ribs">false rib</a>, fracturing it and causing considerable damage to his internal organs, particularly his <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>, before becoming lodged in his first or second <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_vertebrae" title="Lumbar vertebrae">lumbar vertebra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schachner_55-41"><a href="#cite_note-schachner-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:429</sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> Biographer Ron Chernow considered the circumstances to indicate that, after taking deliberate aim, Burr fired second,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-65"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:704</sup> while biographer James Earnest Cooke suggested that Burr took careful aim and shot first, and Hamilton fired while falling, after being struck by Burr's bullet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The paralyzed Hamilton, who knew himself to be mortally wounded, was ferried to the <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">Greenwich Village</a> home of his friend <a href="/wiki/William_Bayard_Jr." title="William Bayard Jr.">William Bayard Jr.</a>, who had been waiting on the dock. After final visits from his family and friends and considerable suffering, Hamilton died on the following afternoon, July 12, 1804, at Bayard's home at what is now 80–82 Jane Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-66"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:705, 708</sup> <a href="/wiki/Gouverneur_Morris" title="Gouverneur Morris">Gouverneur Morris</a> gave the eulogy at his funeral and secretly established a fund to support his widow and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-67"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:712–13, 725</sup> Hamilton was buried in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_Churchyard_Cemetery" title="Trinity Churchyard Cemetery">Trinity Churchyard Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3859" data-file-width="2562" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg/170px-Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg/255px-Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg/340px-Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hamilton_Trumbull_1792-retouch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamilton by <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>, 1792</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While Hamilton was stationed in <a href="/wiki/Morristown,_New_Jersey" title="Morristown, New Jersey">Morristown, New Jersey</a> in the winter of December 1779-March 1780, he met <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Schuyler_Hamilton" title="Elizabeth Schuyler Hamilton">Elizabeth Schuyler</a>, a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Philip_Schuyler" title="Philip Schuyler">Philip Schuyler</a> and Catherine Van Rensselaer. The two were married on December 14, 1780, at the <a href="/wiki/Schuyler_Mansion" title="Schuyler Mansion">Schuyler Mansion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany, New York</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-68"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:128–29</sup> He and Elizabeth had eight children, including two named Philip. The <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hamilton" title="Philip Hamilton">elder Philip</a>, Hamilton's first child (born January 22, 1782), was killed in 1801 in a duel with <a href="/wiki/George_Eacker" title="George Eacker">George I. Eacker</a>, whom he had publicly insulted in a Manhattan theater. The <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hamilton_(the_second)" title="Philip Hamilton (the second)">second Philip</a>, Hamilton's last child, was born on June 2, 1802, after the first Philip was killed. Their other children were Angelica (September 25, 1784 – February 6, 1857), <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_Jr." title="Alexander Hamilton Jr.">Alexander</a> (May 16, 1786 – August 2, 1875), <a href="/wiki/James_Alexander_Hamilton" title="James Alexander Hamilton">James Alexander</a> (April 14, 1788 – September 1878),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Church_Hamilton" title="John Church Hamilton">John Church</a> (August 22, 1792 – July 25, 1882), <a href="/wiki/William_S._Hamilton" title="William S. Hamilton">William Stephen</a> (August 4, 1797 – October 9, 1850), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Eliza_Hamilton_Holly&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Eliza Hamilton Holly (page does not exist)">Eliza</a> (November 26, 1799 – October 17, 1859).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-69"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:582</sup> The second Philip's son was the psychiatrist, <a href="/wiki/Allan_McLane_Hamilton" title="Allan McLane Hamilton">Allan McLane Hamilton</a> <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">FRSE</a></span> (1848–1919).
