After Hamilton’s death in 1804, Elizabeth endeavored to preserve his legacy. She re-organized all of Alexander's letters, papers, and writings with the help of her son, <a href="/wiki/John_Church_Hamilton" title="John Church Hamilton">John Church Hamilton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup> and persevered through many setbacks in getting his biography published. She was so devoted to Alexander's memory that she wore a small package around her neck containing the pieces of a sonnet which Alexander wrote for her during the early days of their courtship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs-wgbh_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs-wgbh-145">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton was also close to Elizabeth's sisters. During his lifetime he was even rumored to have had an affair with his wife's older sister, <a href="/wiki/Angelica_Schuyler_Church" title="Angelica Schuyler Church">Angelica</a>, who 3 years before Hamilton's marriage to Elizabeth had eloped with <a href="/wiki/John_Barker_Church" title="John Barker Church">John Barker Church</a>, an Englishman who made a fortune in North America during the Revolution. She returned with Church to London after the war, where she stayed for over 12 years and later became a joint friend of <a href="/wiki/Maria_Cosway" title="Maria Cosway">Maria Cosway</a> and Thomas Jefferson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-70"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:315</sup> Even though the style of their correspondence was flirtatious, modern historians like Chernow and Fielding agree that despite contemporary gossip there is no conclusive evidence that Hamilton's relationship with Angelica was ever physical or went beyond a strong affinity between in-laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fielding_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fielding-147">[141]</a></sup> Hamilton also maintained a correspondence with Elizabeth's younger sister Margarita, nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Schuyler" title="Peggy Schuyler">Peggy</a>, who was the recipient of his first letters praising her sister Elizabeth at the time of his courtship in early 1780.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hamilton.27s_religion">Hamilton's religion</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton, as a youth in the West Indies, was an orthodox and conventional Presbyterian of the "<a href="/wiki/First_Great_Awakening" title="First Great Awakening">New Light</a>" evangelical type (as opposed to the "Old Light" Calvinists); he was being taught by a student of <a href="/wiki/John_Witherspoon" title="John Witherspoon">John Witherspoon</a>, a moderate of the New School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup> He wrote two or three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hymns" title="Hymns">hymns</a>, which were published in the local newspaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-71"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38</sup> Robert Troup, his college roommate, noted that Hamilton was "in the habit of praying on his knees night and morning."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gordon Wood says that Hamilton dropped his youthful religiosity during the Revolution and became, "a conventional liberal with theistic inclinations who was an irregular churchgoer at best"; however, he returned to religion in his last years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup> Chernow says Hamilton was nominally an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalian</a>, but:</p>
<dl>
<dd>he was not clearly affiliated with the denomination and did not seem to attend church regularly or take communion. Like Adams, Franklin, and Jefferson, Hamilton had probably fallen under the sway of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>, which sought to substitute reason for revelation and dropped the notion of an active God that will intervene in human affairs. At the same time, he never doubted God's existence, embracing Christianity as a system of morality and cosmic justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-72"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:205</sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Stories were circulated that Hamilton had made two quips about God at the time of the Constitutional Convention in 1787.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adair1955_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-adair1955-152">[146]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, he displayed a utilitarian approach to using religion for political ends, such as by maligning Jefferson as "the atheist," and insisting that Christianity and Jeffersonian democracy were incompatible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adair1955_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-adair1955-152">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:316</sup> After 1801, Hamilton further asserted the truth of Christianity; he proposed a Christian Constitutional Society in 1802, to take hold of "some strong feeling of the mind" to elect "<i>fit</i> men" to office, and he wrote of "Christian welfare societies" for the poor. He was not a member of any denomination. After being shot, Hamilton spoke of his belief in God's mercy, and of his desire to renounce dueling; he was given communion by the Episcopal Bishop of New York, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Moore_(bishop)" title="Benjamin Moore (bishop)">Benjamin Moore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[note 7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton had always had respect for Jews. His birthplace of Charlestown had a large Jewish population with whom Hamilton came into contact on a regular basis. As a boy, he had learned <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Hebrew</a> and could recite the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> in its original language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[147]</a></sup> He believed that Jewish achievement was a result of <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">divine providence</a> and warned that those who discredit the Jews "destroy the Christian religion." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-73"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>Hamilton's interpretations of the Constitution set forth in the <i>Federalist Papers</i> remain highly influential, as seen in scholarly studies and court decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the Constitution was ambiguous as to the exact balance of power between national and state governments, Hamilton consistently took the side of greater federal power at the expense of the states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[149]</a></sup> As Secretary of the Treasury, he established—against the intense opposition of Secretary of State Jefferson—the country's first national bank. Hamilton justified the creation of this bank, and other increased federal powers, under Congress's constitutional powers to issue currency, to regulate interstate commerce, and to do anything else that would be "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_and_proper_clause" title="Necessary and proper clause">necessary and proper</a>" to enact the provisions of the Constitution. Jefferson, on the other hand, took a stricter view of the Constitution: parsing the text carefully, he found no specific authorization for a national bank. This controversy was eventually settled by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/McCulloch_v._Maryland" title="McCulloch v. Maryland">McCulloch v. Maryland</a></i>, which in essence adopted Hamilton's view, granting the federal government broad freedom to select the best means to execute its constitutionally enumerated powers, specifically the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Implied_powers" title="Implied powers">implied powers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[150]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_Era" title="Progressive Era">Progressive Era</a> demonstrated the sorts of crises and politics Hamilton's administrative republic sought to avoid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tulis1987_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tulis1987-158">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton's policies as Secretary of the Treasury greatly affected the United States government and still continue to influence it. His constitutional interpretation, specifically of the <a href="/wiki/Necessary_and_Proper_Clause" title="Necessary and Proper Clause">Necessary and Proper Clause</a>, set precedents for federal authority that are still used by the courts and are considered an authority on constitutional interpretation. The prominent French diplomat <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Charles Maurice de Talleyrand</a>, who spent 1794 in the United States, wrote, "I consider <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_James_Fox" title="Charles James Fox">Fox</a>, and Hamilton the three greatest men of our epoch, and if I were forced to decide between the three, I would give without hesitation the first place to Hamilton", adding that Hamilton had intuited the problems of European conservatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Opinions of Hamilton have run the gamut: both John Adams and Thomas Jefferson viewed him as unprincipled and dangerously aristocratic. Hamilton's reputation was mostly negative in the eras of <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a>. However, by the Progressive era, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Croly" title="Herbert Croly">Herbert Croly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> praised his leadership of a strong government. Several nineteenth- and twentieth-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> entered politics by writing laudatory biographies of Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians have generally taken one of two main views of Hamilton. Wilentz says:</p>
<dl>
<dd>In recent years, Hamilton and his reputation have decidedly gained the initiative among scholars who portray him as the visionary architect of the modern liberal capitalist economy and of a dynamic federal government headed by an energetic executive. Jefferson and his allies, by contrast, have come across as naïve, dreamy idealists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[154]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The older Jeffersonian view attacks him as a centralizer, to the point sometimes of advocating monarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-74"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:397–98</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monuments_and_memorials">Monuments and memorials</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portraits_on_currency_and_postage_stamps">Portraits on currency and postage stamps</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="7376" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg/220px-US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg/330px-US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg/440px-US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US10dollarbill-Series_2004A.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hamilton on the Series 2004A U.S. $10 bill</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-$5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="545" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg/120px-Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg/180px-Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg/240px-Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-%245.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton3_1956_Issue-$5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1956 $5 Hamilton postage stamp</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Hamilton has been <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_on_United_States_banknotes" title="List of people on United States banknotes">depicted</a> on more denominations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_currency" title="United States currency">U.S. currency</a> than anyone else. He has appeared on the <a href="/wiki/File:US-$2-LT-1862-Fr-41.jpg" title="File:US-$2-LT-1862-Fr-41.jpg">$2</a>, <a href="/wiki/File:US-$5-DN-1861-Fr.1.jpg" title="File:US-$5-DN-1861-Fr.1.jpg">$5</a>, <a href="/wiki/File:US-$10-FRN-1934-A-Fr.2303.jpg" title="File:US-$10-FRN-1934-A-Fr.2303.jpg">$10</a>, <a href="/wiki/File:US-$20-LT-1875-Fr-128.jpg" title="File:US-$20-LT-1875-Fr-128.jpg">$20</a>, <a href="/wiki/File:US-$50-LT-1862-Fr-148a.jpg" title="File:US-$50-LT-1862-Fr-148a.jpg">$50</a>, and <a href="/wiki/File:US-$1000-GC-1882-Fr.1218g.jpg" title="File:US-$1000-GC-1882-Fr.1218g.jpg">$1,000</a> notes. Hamilton also appears on the $500 Series EE Savings Bond.</p>
<p>Hamilton's portrait has been featured on the front of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_ten_dollar_bill" title="US ten dollar bill">U.S. $10 bill</a> since 1928. The source of the engraving is <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>'s 1805 portrait of Hamilton, in the portrait collection of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Hall" title="New York City Hall">New York City Hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[155]</a></sup> In June 2015, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" title="United States Treasury">U.S. Treasury</a> announced a decision to replace the engraving of Hamilton with that of a woman; however, before the bill was actually redesigned, the decision was changed due to the unanticipated popular success of the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> musical <i><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_(musical)" title="Hamilton (musical)">Hamilton</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo-tubman_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-tubman-163">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first postage stamp to honor Hamilton was issued by the U.S. Post Office in 1870. The portrayals on the 1870 and 1888 issues are from the same engraved die, which was modeled after a <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(Ceracchi)" title="Alexander Hamilton (Ceracchi)">bust of Hamilton</a> by Italian sculptor <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Ceracchi" title="Giuseppe Ceracchi">Giuseppe Ceracchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[157]</a></sup> The Hamilton 1870 issue was the first U.S. postage stamp to honor a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a>. The three-cent red commemorative issue, which was released on the 200th anniversary of Hamilton's birth in 1957, includes a rendition of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Hall#Historic_building" title="Federal Hall">Federal Hall</a> building, located in New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[158]</a></sup> On March 19, 1956, the United States Postal Service issued the $5 <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Issue" title="Liberty Issue">Liberty Issue</a> postage stamp honoring Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Grange">The Grange</span></h4>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads,_Central_Park,_NYC_-_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1503" data-file-width="1002" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg/160px-Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg/240px-Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg/320px-Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads%2C_Central_Park%2C_NYC_-_02.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Hamilton_by_Conrads,_Central_Park,_NYC_-_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(Conrads)" title="Alexander Hamilton (Conrads)">Hamilton statue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Grange_National_Memorial" title="Hamilton Grange National Memorial">The Grange</a> is the only home Alexander Hamilton ever owned. It is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federal_style" title="Federal style">Federal style</a> mansion designed by <a href="/wiki/John_McComb_Jr." title="John McComb Jr.">John McComb Jr.</a>. It was built on Hamilton's 32-acre country estate in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamilton_Heights" title="Hamilton Heights">Hamilton Heights</a> in upper Manhattan, and was completed in 1802. Hamilton named the house "The Grange" after the estate of his grandfather Alexander in <a href="/wiki/Ayrshire" title="Ayrshire">Ayrshire</a>, Scotland. The house remained in the family until 1833, when his widow Eliza sold it to Thomas E. Davis, a British-born real estate developer, for $25,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HamHolly_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-HamHolly-167">[160]</a></sup> Part of the proceeds were used by Eliza to purchase a new townhouse from Davis in <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">Greenwich Village</a> (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Hamilton-Holly_House" title="Hamilton-Holly House">Hamilton-Holly House</a>, where Eliza lived until 1843 with her grown children Alexander and Eliza, and their spouses).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HamHolly_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-HamHolly-167">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Grange was first moved from its original location in 1889, and was moved again in 2008 to a spot in <a href="/wiki/St._Nicholas_Park" title="St. Nicholas Park">St. Nicholas Park</a> in Hamilton Heights, on land that was once part of the Hamilton estate. The historic structure, now designated as the <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Grange_National_Memorial" title="Hamilton Grange National Memorial">Hamilton Grange National Memorial</a>, was restored to its original 1802 appearance in 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[161]</a></sup> and is maintained by the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleges_and_universities">Colleges and universities</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, Hamilton's alma mater, has official memorials to Hamilton on its campus in New York City. The college's main classroom building for the humanities is <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Hall_(Columbia_University)" title="Hamilton Hall (Columbia University)">Hamilton Hall</a>, and a large statue of Hamilton stands in front of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[166]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University_Press" title="Columbia University Press">university press</a> has published his complete works in a multivolume <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Letterpress" title="Letterpress">letterpress</a> edition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[167]</a></sup> Columbia University's student group for ROTC cadets and Marine officer candidates is named the Alexander Hamilton Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton served as one of the first trustees of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamilton-Oneida_Academy" title="Hamilton-Oneida Academy">Hamilton-Oneida Academy</a> in Clinton, New York, which was renamed <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_College_(New_York)" title="Hamilton College (New York)">Hamilton College</a> in 1812, after receiving a college charter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main administration building of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coast_Guard_Academy" title="Coast Guard Academy">Coast Guard Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_London,_Connecticut" title="New London, Connecticut">New London, Connecticut</a>, is named Hamilton Hall to commemorate Hamilton's creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Revenue_Cutter_Service" title="United States Revenue Cutter Service">United States Revenue Cutter Service</a>, one of the predecessor services of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buildings_and_public_art">Buildings and public art</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:172px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:234px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:-30px; top:-48px; width:210px; position:absolute;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg" title="Statue of Hamilton at the U.S. Treasury"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/210px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/315px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/420px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;"></div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/210px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/315px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3a/JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg/420px-JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JEFraser_Hamilton.jpg" title="File:JEFraser Hamilton.jpg"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(Fraser)" title="Alexander Hamilton (Fraser)">Statue of Hamilton</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Building_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Treasury Building (Washington, D.C.)">U.S. Treasury</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1880, Hamilton's son <a href="/wiki/John_Church_Hamilton" title="John Church Hamilton">John Church Hamilton</a> commissioned <a href="/wiki/Carl_Conrads" title="Carl Conrads">Carl Conrads</a> to sculpt a granite <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(Conrads)" title="Alexander Hamilton (Conrads)">statue</a>, now located in <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a>, New York City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>A bronze statue of Hamilton by <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Simmons" title="Franklin Simmons">Franklin Simmons</a>, dated 1905–06, overlooks the <a href="/wiki/Great_Falls_(Passaic_River)" title="Great Falls (Passaic River)">Great Falls</a> of the Passaic River at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paterson_Great_Falls_National_Historical_Park" title="Paterson Great Falls National Historical Park">Paterson Great Falls National Historical Park</a> in New Jersey.</p>
<p>In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_U.S._Custom_House" title="Alexander Hamilton U.S. Custom House">U.S. Custom House</a> in New York City was renamed after Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>The U.S. Army's <a href="/wiki/Fort_Hamilton" title="Fort Hamilton">Fort Hamilton</a> in Brooklyn is named after Hamilton.</p>
<p>In Washington, D.C., the south terrace of the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Building_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Treasury Building (Washington, D.C.)">Treasury Building</a> features a <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(Fraser)" title="Alexander Hamilton (Fraser)">statue of Hamilton</a> by <a href="/wiki/James_Earle_Fraser_(sculptor)" title="James Earle Fraser (sculptor)">James Earle Fraser</a>, which was dedicated on May 17, 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Chicago, a thirteen-foot tall statue of Hamilton by sculptor <a href="/wiki/John_Angel_(sculptor)" title="John Angel (sculptor)">John Angel</a> was cast in 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suntimes_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-suntimes-182">[175]</a></sup> It was not installed at <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park" title="Lincoln Park">Lincoln Park</a> until 1952, due to problems with a <a href="/wiki/John_Angel_(sculptor)#Chicago_Hamilton_statue" title="John Angel (sculptor)">controversial 78-foot tall columned shelter</a> designed for it and later demolished in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suntimes_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-suntimes-182">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chicago-183">[176]</a></sup> The statue has remained on public display, and was restored and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilded" title="Gilded">regilded</a> in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suntimes_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-suntimes-182">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>A bronze sculpture of Hamilton titled <i>The American Cape</i>, by Kristen Visbal, was unveiled at Journal Square in downtown <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ohio" title="Hamilton, Ohio">Hamilton, Ohio</a>, in October 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohio_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-ohio-184">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nevis_Historical_and_Conservation_Society#The_Alexander_Hamilton_Museum" title="Nevis Historical and Conservation Society">The Alexander Hamilton Museum</a> is in a Georgian house in Charlestown, Nevis Island. The present building was built on the foundations of the house where Hamilton was born and where he lived during his childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[178]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_sites">Geographic sites</span></h4>
<p>Numerous American towns and cities, including <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Kansas" title="Hamilton, Kansas">Hamilton, Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Missouri" title="Hamilton, Missouri">Hamilton, Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Massachusetts" title="Hamilton, Massachusetts">Hamilton, Massachusetts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ohio" title="Hamilton, Ohio">Hamilton, Ohio</a>, were named in honor of Alexander Hamilton. In eight states, counties have been named for Hamilton:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ham-gen_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-ham-gen-186">[179]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 18em; -webkit-column-width: 18em; column-width: 18em;">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Florida" title="Hamilton County, Florida">Hamilton County, Florida</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Illinois" title="Hamilton County, Illinois">Hamilton County, Illinois</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Indiana" title="Hamilton County, Indiana">Hamilton County, Indiana</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Kansas" title="Hamilton County, Kansas">Hamilton County, Kansas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Nebraska" title="Hamilton County, Nebraska">Hamilton County, Nebraska</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_New_York" title="Hamilton County, New York">Hamilton County, New York</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Ohio" title="Hamilton County, Ohio">Hamilton County, Ohio</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_County,_Tennessee" title="Hamilton County, Tennessee">Hamilton County, Tennessee</a></li>
</ul>
</div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_slavery">On slavery</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_and_slavery" title="Alexander Hamilton and slavery">Alexander Hamilton and slavery</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/New_York_Manumission_Society" title="New York Manumission Society">New York Manumission Society</a></div>
<p>Hamilton was active during the Revolution in trying to raise black troops for the army, with the promise of freedom. At a time when most white leaders doubted the capacity of blacks, Hamilton believed slavery was morally wrong and wrote, "their natural faculties are as good as ours."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[180]</a></sup> In 1785 he followed the lead of his close associate <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> in founding the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Manumission_Society" title="New York Manumission Society">New-York Society for Promoting the Manumission of Slaves, and Protecting Such of Them as Have Been, or May be Liberated.</a>, the main anti-slavery organization in New York. It successfully promoted the abolition of the international slave trade in New York City and (shortly after his death) passed a state law to end slavery in New York and gradually free its slaves. In the 1780s and 1790s he generally opposed pro-slavery southern interests. However he did occasionally purchase or sell domestic servants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_economics">On economics</span></h3>
<p>Hamilton has been portrayed as the "patron saint" of the <a href="/wiki/American_School_(economics)" title="American School (economics)">American School</a> of economic philosophy that, according to one historian, dominated economic policy after 1861.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[182]</a></sup>  He firmly supported government intervention in favor of business, after the manner of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert">Jean-Baptiste Colbert</a>, as early as the fall of 1781.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chernow_3-75"><a href="#cite_note-chernow-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:170</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[184]</a></sup>  Hamilton opposed the British ideas of <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, which he believed skewed benefits to colonial and imperial powers, in favor of <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>, which he believed would help develop the fledgling nation's emerging economy.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_C._Carey" title="Henry C. Carey">Henry C. Carey</a> was inspired by his writings.  Hamilton influenced the ideas and work of the German <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_List" title="Friedrich List">Friedrich List</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[185]</a></sup>  In Hamilton's view, a strong executive, linked to the support of the people, could become the linchpin of an administrative republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999122_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999122-193">[186]</a></sup>  The dominance of executive leadership in the formulation and carrying out of policy was essential to resist the deterioration of republican government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[187]</a></sup>  Ian Patrick Austin has explored the similarities between Hamiltonian recommendations and the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_Japan" title="Meiji Japan">Meiji Japan</a> after 1860.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2345" data-file-width="3013" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg/220px-Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg/330px-Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg/440px-Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lin-Manuel_Miranda_in_Hamilton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lin-Manuel Miranda as Alexander Hamilton in the musical <i>Hamilton</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Apart from the $10 bill, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_(play)" title="Hamilton (play)">a 1917 play</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_(film)" title="Alexander Hamilton (film)">1931 film</a>, Hamilton did not attract much attention in American popular culture<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[189]</a></sup> until the advent of the 2015 hit <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theatre" title="Broadway theatre">Broadway</a> musical <i><a href="/wiki/Hamilton_(musical)" title="Hamilton (musical)">Hamilton</a></i>. The musical, which features music, lyrics, and a book by <a href="/wiki/Lin-Manuel_Miranda" title="Lin-Manuel Miranda">Lin-Manuel Miranda</a>, is based on a biography by <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> called the show "an achievement of historical and cultural reimagining. In Miranda's telling, the headlong rise of one self-made immigrant becomes the story of America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[190]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Off-Broadway" title="Off-Broadway">off-Broadway</a> production of <i>Hamilton</i> won the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Drama_Desk_Award_for_Outstanding_Musical" title="Drama Desk Award for Outstanding Musical">Drama Desk Award for Outstanding Musical</a> as well as seven other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drama_Desk_Awards" title="Drama Desk Awards">Drama Desk Awards</a>.  In 2016, <i>Hamilton</i> received the <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize_for_Drama" title="Pulitzer Prize for Drama">Pulitzer Prize for Drama</a> and a record 16 Tony nominations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyti-Ham_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyti-Ham-198">[191]</a></sup> winning 11 of them including <a href="/wiki/Tony_Award_for_Best_Musical" title="Tony Award for Best Musical">Best Musical</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton has also appeared as a significant figure in popular works focusing on other American political figures of his time. He is a major character in <a href="/wiki/Gore_Vidal" title="Gore Vidal">Gore Vidal</a>'s 1973 historical novel <i><a href="/wiki/Burr_(novel)" title="Burr (novel)">Burr</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[194]</a></sup> and in episodes of the 1976 <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adams_Chronicles" title="The Adams Chronicles">The Adams Chronicles</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[195]</a></sup> Hamilton is a major villain in <a href="/wiki/L._Neil_Smith" title="L. Neil Smith">L. Neil Smith</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libertarian" title="Libertarian">libertarian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alternative_history" title="Alternative history">alternative history</a> series "<a href="/wiki/North_American_Confederacy" title="North American Confederacy">North American Confederacy</a>".</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hamilton's spelling "Lavien" may be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sephardic" title="Sephardic">Sephardic</a> version of "Levine". The couple may have lived apart from one another under an order of legal separation, with Faucette as the guilty party, meaning that her remarriage was not permitted on St. Croix.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Jefferson wrote years afterward that Hamilton had a discussion with him around this time period about the capital of the United States being relocated to Virginia by mean of a 'pill' that "would be peculiarly bitter to the Southern States, and that some concomitant measure should be adopted to sweeten it a little to them".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The System of Revenue Cutters was also known as the <i>Revenue Service</i>, <i>Revenue-Marine Service</i>, and <i>System of Cutters</i> after being enacted by Congress. It officially became the Coast Guard in 1915.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Quote: <i>I see by a paper of last evening that even in New York a meeting of the people has taken place, at the instance of the Republican party, and that a committee is appointed for the like purpose.</i> See also Smith, (2004) p.832.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The May 1800 election chose the New York legislature, which would in turn choose electors; Burr had won this by making it a referendum on the presidency, and by persuading better-qualified candidates to run, who declared their candidacy only after the Federalists had announced their ticket. Hamilton asked Jay and the lame-duck legislature to pass a law declaring a special federal election, in which each district would choose an elector. He also supplied a map, with as many Federalist districts as possible.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hamilton had given his son Philip the same advice in his duel with George I. Eacker in 1801 that resulted in Philip's death. The maneuver of throwing shots on the field of honor was referred to as <i>delope</i> by the French. (Chernow, p. 653)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Adair and Harvey, "Christian Statesman?"; Quotes on the Christian Constitutional Society are from Hamilton's letter to James A. Bayard of April 1802, quoted by Adair and Harvey. McDonald, says p. 356, that Hamilton's faith "had not entirely departed" him before the crisis of 1801.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li><cite class="citation book">Wood, Gordon S. (2009). <i>Empire of Liberty: A History of the Early Republic, 1789–1815</i>. New York: Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-503914-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-503914-6">978-0-19-503914-6</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+Hamilton&amp;rft.aufirst=Gordon+S.&amp;rft.aulast=Wood&amp;rft.btitle=Empire+of+Liberty%3A+A+History+of+the+Early+Republic%2C+1789%931815&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-503914-6&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> The most recent synthesis of the era.</li>
<li><cite class="citation book">Wright, Robert E. (2002). <i>Hamilton Unbound: Finance and the Creation of the American Republic</i>. Westport: Greenwood Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-313-32397-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-313-32397-6">0-313-32397-6</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+Hamilton&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Wright&amp;rft.btitle=Hamilton+Unbound%3A+Finance+and+the+Creation+of+the+American+Republic&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-313-32397-6&amp;rft.place=Westport&amp;rft.pub=Greenwood+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Cooke, Jacob E., ed. <i>Alexander Hamilton: A Profile</i>. 1967. (Short excerpts from AH and his critics)</li>
<li>Cunningham, Noble E. <i>Jefferson vs. Hamilton: Confrontations that Shaped a Nation</i>. 2000. (Short collection of primary sources, with commentary)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federalist_Papers" title="Federalist Papers">Federalist Papers</a>. Under the shared pseudonym "Publius". By Alexander Hamilton (c. 52 articles), <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> (28 articles), and <a href="/wiki/John_Jay" title="John Jay">John Jay</a> (five articles).</li>
<li>Freeman, Joanne B., ed. <i>Alexander Hamilton: Writings</i>. 2001, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781931082044">ISBN 978-1-931082-04-4</a>. <a class="external text" href="http://www.loa.org/" rel="nofollow">The Library of America</a> edition, 1108 pages. (All of Hamilton's major writings and many of his letters)</li>
<li>Frisch, Morton J., ed. <i>Selected Writings and Speeches of Alexander Hamilton.</i> 1985.</li>
<li>Hamilton, Alexander; Hamilton, John Church. <i>The Works of Alexander Hamilton: Miscellanies, 1789–1795: France; Duties on imports; National bank; Manufactures; Revenue circulars; Reports on claims, etc</i>. 1850. John F. Trow, Printer. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=75YoAQAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA149" rel="nofollow">free online EBook edition</a></li>
<li>Goebel, Julius, Jr., and Joseph H. Smith, eds. <i>The Law Practice of Alexander Hamilton</i>. 5 vols. Columbia University Press, 1964–80. (The legal counterpart to <i>The Papers of Alexander Hamilton</i>)</li>
<li>Lodge, Henry Cabot, ed. <i>The Works of Alexander Hamilton</i>. 10 vols. 1904. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/worksalexanderh10hamigoog" rel="nofollow">full text online at Internet Archive</a> <a class="external text" href="http://oll.libertyfund.org/?option=com_staticxt&amp;staticfile=show.php%3Fperson=201&amp;Itemid=28" rel="nofollow">online in HTML edition</a>. (The only online collection of Hamilton's writings and letters, containing about 1.3 million words)</li>
<li>Morris, Richard, ed. <i>Alexander Hamilton and the Founding of the Nation</i>. 1957. (Excerpts from AH's writings)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Report_on_Manufactures" title="Report on Manufactures">Report on Manufactures</a>. (AH's economic program for the United States)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Report_on_Public_Credit" title="Report on Public Credit">Report on Public Credit</a>. (AH's financial program for the United States)</li>
<li>Syrett, Harold C., Jacob E. Cooke, and Barbara Chernow, eds. <i>The Papers of Alexander Hamilton</i>. 27 vols. Columbia University Press, 1961–87. (Includes all letters and writings by Hamilton, and all important letters written to him; this is the definitive edition of Hamilton's works, intensively annotated)</li>
<li>Taylor, George Rogers, ed. <i>Hamilton and the National Debt</i>. 1950. (Excerpts from all sides in the 1790s)</li>
</ul>
